"Transportation Research Part B: MethodologicalThe two-train separation problem on non-level track\u2014driving strategies that minimize total required tractive energy subject to prescribed section clearance timesAbstract When two trains travel along the same track in the same direction it is a common safety requirement with three-aspect signalling that the trains must be separated by at least two signals if they wish to continue following the planned schedule. Under these separation conditions there will always be at least one clear section of track between the two trains. When these conditions are violated the following train must adopt a revised strategy that will enable it to stop at the next signal if required. In this paper we find necessary conditions on non-level track to minimize the total tractive energy required for both trains to complete their respective journeys within the allowed time subject to safety-compatible separation constraints in the form of a prescribed set of latest allowed section exit times for the leading train and a corresponding prescribed set of earliest allowed section entry times for the following train. We use classical methods of constrained optimzation to show that the optimal driving strategy for each train is defined by a unique optimal driving speed on each section and that the sequence of optimal driving speeds is a decreasing sequence for the leading train and an increasing sequence for the following train. We illustrate our results by finding optimal strategies and associated speed profiles for both the leading train and the following train in some elementary but realistic examples.serial JL 271728 291210 291772 291817 31 Transportation Research Part B: Methodological TRANSPORTATIONRESEARCHPARTBMETHODOLOGICAL 2018-03-27 2018-03-27 2018-03-27 2018-03-27 2019-01-26T04:45:45 1-s2.0-S019126151730807X S0191-2615(17)30807-X S019126151730807X 10.1016/j.trb.2018.03.012 S300 S300.3 FULL-TEXT 1-s2.0-S0191261518X00046 2019-01-26T05:12:43.88563Z 0 0 20180501 20180531 2018 2018-03-27T16:35:15.605483Z articleinfo articletitlenorm authfirstinitialnorm authfirstsurnamenorm cid cids contenttype copyright crossmark dateloaded dateloadedtxt datesearch datesort dateupdated dco docsubtype doctype doi eid ewtransactionid hubeid indexeddate issn issnnorm itemstage itemtransactionid itemweight openaccess openarchive pg pgfirst pglast pii piinorm pubdateend pubdatestart pubdatetxt pubyr sectiontitle sortorder srctitle srctitlenorm srctype ssids alllist content subj subheadings suppl tomb volfirst volissue volumelist webpdf webpdfpagecount yearnav figure body mmlmath acknowledge affil articletitle auth authfirstini authfull authkeywords authlast grantnumber grantsponsor grantsponsorid highlightsabst orcid otherkwds primabst ref 0191-2615 01912615 true 111 111 C Volume 111 8 135 167 135 167 201805 May 2018 2018-05-01 2018-05-31 2018 Research papers article fla Crown Copyright \u00a9 2018 Published by Elsevier Ltd. Fig. 5 The two-train separation problem on non-level track\u2014driving strategies that minimize total required tractive energy subject to prescribed section clearance times Amie Albrecht Phil Howlett \u204e phil.howlett@unisa.edu.au Peter Pudney Xuan Vu Peng Zhou Scheduling and Control Group, Centre for Industrial and Applied Mathematics, School of Information Technology and Mathematical Sciences, University of South Australia, Mawson Lakes 5095, Australia Scheduling and Control Group, Centre for Industrial and Applied Mathematics, School of Information Technology and Mathematical Sciences University of South Australia Mawson Lakes 5095 Australia \u204e Corresponding author. Highlights \u2022 We formulate and solve a theoretical two-train separation problem on non-level track. \u2022 We find optimal driving strategies for the leading and following trains while maintaining safe separation. \u2022 We present examples to differentiate unrestricted optimal strategies and optimal strategies with safe separation. \u2022 We show that the proposed strategies can be implemented with a three-aspect signalling system. \u2022 We show that the methods can be extended to separation of more than two trains. Abstract When two trains travel along the same track in the same direction it is a common safety requirement with three-aspect signalling that the trains must be separated by at least two signals if they wish to continue following the planned schedule. Under these separation conditions there will always be at least one clear section of track between the two trains. When these conditions are violated the following train must adopt a revised strategy that will enable it to stop at the next signal if required. In this paper we find necessary conditions on non-level track to minimize the total tractive energy required for both trains to complete their respective journeys within the allowed time subject to safety-compatible separation constraints in the form of a prescribed set of latest allowed section exit times for the leading train and a corresponding prescribed set of earliest allowed section entry times for the following train. We use classical methods of constrained optimzation to show that the optimal driving strategy for each train is defined by a unique optimal driving speed on each section and that the sequence of optimal driving speeds is a decreasing sequence for the leading train and an increasing sequence for the following train. We illustrate our results by finding optimal strategies and associated speed profiles for both the leading train and the following train in some elementary but realistic examples. Keywords Optimal train control Train separation Intermediate time constraints Tractive energy minimization MSC 49K15 49K20 49MO5 49N90 90B06 1 Introduction When two trains travel along the same track in the same direction it is a common safety requirement with three-aspect signalling that the trains should be separated by at least two signals if they wish to continue following the planned schedule. If the signals are located at fixed positions along the track then they divide the track into different sections. The separation constraints now mean that the following train cannot enter a particular section using the planned driving strategy unless the leading train has left the subsequent section. In this paper we find driving strategies for the leading train and the following train on non-level track that minimize total tractive energy consumption and allow the trains to complete their journeys at the specified times subject to a prescribed set of intermediate section clearance times. The prescribed clearance times must enforce the separation constraints described above. We use previous work on optimal driving strategies for a single train (Albrecht et al., 2015a; 2015b; Howlett, 2000; Khmelnitsky, 2000; Liu and Golovitcher, 2003) and a new application of the Pontryagin principle to solve the two-train separation problem on non-level track. The new results provide a substantial extension of our recent solution to the corresponding two-train separation problem on level track (Albrecht et al., 2015c; 2015d; 2015e). Our first aim is to find general forms for the optimal speed profiles of both the leading train and following train when journey times are fixed and when latest allowed section exit times for the leading train and earliest allowed section entry times for the following train are prescribed by the network operator. Our second aim is find the precise control functions and corresponding speed profiles that minimize total energy consumption subject to the prescribed section clearance times and allow both trains to complete their journeys on time. The method we describe can be used with sections of any length and can also be applied to situations where more than two clear sections are required between trains to ensure that both trains can continue to follow the planned schedule. However in this paper we will simply insist that the following train cannot enter a particular section using the planned driving strategy until the leading train has left the subsequent section. This separation can be achieved by specifying sets of intermediate section clearance times for each train. We assume that the total journey time is specified for each train. We suppose that the latest allowed section exit times for the leading train are specified by a vector h \u2113 = { h \u2113 , j } j = 1 n and that the earliest allowed section entry times for the following train are specified by a vector h f = { h f , j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 . We wish to find feasible controls u = ( u \u2113 , u f ) \u2208 U ( h ) and associated speed profiles v = ( v \u2113 , v f ) for both the leading train and the following train that minimize the total tractive energy J ( u , h ) consumed by the trains subject to the vector h = ( h \u2113 , h f ) of prescribed clearance times. That is, we want to find u h so that J ( h ) = J ( u h , h ) = min { J ( u , h ) \u2223 u \u2208 U ( h ) } . A related mathematical problem, which we will address in a subsequent paper, is to find necessary conditions for a vector h 0 of optimal prescribed section clearance times that minimizes the total tractive energy J ( h ) consumed by the two trains over all possible vectors h \u2208 H in the collection of all feasible sets of prescribed section clearance times. Thus J 0 = J ( h 0 ) = min { J ( h ) \u2223 h \u2208 H } . On level track such necessary conditions are known (Albrecht et al., 2015c; 2015d; 2015e) and have been used in a typical application to identify a vector h 0 of optimal specified section clearance times. It seems likely that the derivation of the necessary conditions for optimality on non-level track will be formally the same as the derivation on level track in Albrecht et al. (2015c, 2015d, 2015e) but that the mathematical formul\u00e6 for the required derivatives will be much more complicated. Our ultimate aim is to construct energy-efficient timetables for a fleet of trains subject to safety-compatible separation constraints with a driving strategy specified for each train. 1.1 A preview of the main results We show that the optimal strategy for each train is completely determined by the total allowed journey time and the specified section clearance times. This means that the optimal strategy for one train can be computed without any specific knowledge of the optimal strategy for the other. The logical independence of the two optimal strategies is a key observation. We prove that the optimal strategy for each train is defined by a unique optimal driving speed on each section and that the sequence of optimal driving speeds is a decreasing sequence for the leading train and an increasing sequence for the following train. This confirms a natural intuition that early in the journey the leading train should go faster while the following train should go slower. 1.2 Implementation of optimal strategies in a safe-operating environment In modern rail networks train movements are planned to follow carefully-designed timetables that allow coordinated operation of the entire network. Drivers are encouraged to use energy-efficient strategies but are nevertheless expected to reach each scheduled location at a predetermined time or within a prescribed time-window. Despite these expectations network safety is paramount and drivers must always comply with safe-operating instructions from the signalling system. A safe-operating environment has traditionally been enabled using a system of trackside signals in fixed locations. A three-aspect signalling system shows a green light if the next two sections of track are clear; a yellow light if the next section is clear but the section after next is occupied; and a red light if the next section is occupied. A driver will continue following the planned schedule when the train passes a green signal but if the train passes a yellow signal then the driver must switch to a modified speed profile that will enable the train to stop at the next signal if that signal remains red. A driver must not allow the train to pass a red signal. When two or more trains travel in the same direction on the same track in close succession\u2014as often happens on busy suburban passenger lines or dedicated freight networks\u2014the signalling system will allow train drivers to follow the planned schedule only if the separation conditions are maintained. Many different methods are used to determine feasible schedules and in recent times operators have placed increasing emphasis on timetables that allow energy-efficient driving strategies. Whatever method is used to set section clearance times it is desirable to find driving strategies for each train that maintain the required separation and minimize the total tractive energy consumption. Our method will do this, and the necessary conditions for an optimal strategy that we derive will allow the development of algorithms that can calculate optimal driving strategies in real time. 1.3 Train separation with scheduled stopping points Although we do not explicitly consider journeys with scheduled stopping points in our mathematical analysis the method we propose can easily be extended to handle intermediate stopping points as we shall show later in a specific example. We stated earlier that the optimal strategy and associated speed profile for each train is completely determined by the total journey time and the intermediate section clearance times. Therefore the optimal strategy and associated speed profile for each train can be computed with no specific knowledge of the optimal strategy for the other train. Consider the strategy for the leading train. Suppose there are scheduled intermediate stopping points. Since the optimal strategy depends only on the specified section clearance times we can use the intermediate stopping points to divide the original journey into separate sub-journeys within which there are no intermediate stopping points. Now we can apply our proposed method to find the optimal strategy on each sub-journey subject to the specified intermediate section clearance times for the sub-journey. Hence the optimal strategy for each sub-journey is now uniquely defined by a decreasing sequence of optimal driving speeds. The succession of optimal strategies for each sub-journey yields the optimal strategy for the entire journey. Similar remarks apply to the following train with one minor additional requirement. Once again we use the stopping points for the following train\u2014which need not be the same as the stopping points for the leading train\u2014to define separate sub-journeys with no intermediate stopping points. If we introduce a maximum allowed time for each sub-journey then the following train problem can be solved by our proposed method for each sub-journey subject to the prescribed intermediate clearance times. For instance, if xs is a stopping point for the following train then the existing constraint at xs is an earliest allowed entry time to the section ( x s , x s + 1 ) . Since xs is now the endpoint of a sub-journey we impose an additional constraint in the form of a latest allowed exit time from the section ( x s \u2212 1 , x s ) for the sub-journey that finishes at xs . Hence the sub-journey is now specified in standard form and the optimal strategy will be uniquely defined by an increasing sequence of optimal driving speeds. If we prescribe maximum allowed times for all separate sub-journeys then the succession of optimal strategies for each sub-journey defines the optimal strategy for the whole journey. 1.4 The role of the train parameters For both the leading train and the following train the precise details of the optimal strategies\u2014the optimal driving speeds on each section and the associated optimal switching points\u2014depend on the train performance. However the logical independence of the leading and following train problems means that the optimal strategies can be computed separately. Train performance is defined by parameters H for acceleration and K for braking, and by the resistance r = r ( v ) where v denotes the speed of the train. As long as the clearance times are feasible for each train we can use one set of parameters (H \u2113, K \u2113, r \u2113(v)) for the leading train and another set (Hf, Kf, rf (v)) for the following train without changing the general structure of the two optimal strategies. Nevertheless, for the most part, we will assume that the two trains are identical. This is not necessary and is simply a matter of algebraic and notational convenience. It means we can use a generic set of parameters (H, K, r(v)) throughout without causing confusion. 1.5 Notation It is convenient to summarize our notation. \u2022 We consider a rail track with position x\u202f\u2208\u202f[0, X] and signal locations { x j } j = 0 n such that 0 = x 0 < \u22ef < x n = X . We assume the gradient acceleration g(x) is piecewise continuous on the interval [0, X]. The speed of the train is denoted by v = v ( x ) and the resistive acceleration is denoted by r(v). We make repeated use of the related functions \u03c6 ( v ) = v r ( v ) and \u03c8 ( v ) = v 2 r \u2032 ( v ) . For v\u202f>\u202f0 we assume that \u03c6(v) is positive and strictly convex and that \u03c8(v) is positive and strictly increasing. See Albrecht et al. (2015a) and Howlett and Jiaxing (1997) for more details. It is convenient to define the acceleration u = u ( x ) as the difference of two non-negative control functions. We write u = u a \u2212 u b where u a = ( u + | u | ) / 2 \u2265 0 is the driving control and u b = ( \u2212 u + | u | ) / 2 \u2265 0 is the braking control. The control vector u = u ( x ) is defined by setting u = ( u a , u b ) . The cost of a journey J = J ( u ) is the total energy consumed by the driving control minus the energy recovered (if any) from regenerative braking control. \u2022 The trains travel from x 0 = 0 to x n = X . \u2022 The speed and time functions ( v \u2113 , t \u2113 ) = ( v \u2113 ( x ) , t \u2113 ( x ) ) for the leading train and ( v f , t f ) = ( v f ( x ) , t f ( x ) ) for the following train are defined as functions of position by the equations of motion and the initial conditions. \u2022 The leading train starts at time t \u2113 ( 0 ) = 0 and reaches location x at time t \u2113(x). The elapsed journey time for the leading train is t \u2113 ( x ) \u2212 t \u2113 ( 0 ) = t \u2113 ( x ) . The following train starts at time t f ( 0 ) = \u0394 T and reaches location x at time tf (x). The elapsed journey time for the following train is t f ( x ) \u2212 t f ( 0 ) = t f ( x ) \u2212 \u0394 T . The times T\u202f>\u202f0 and \u0394T\u202f>\u202f0 are prescribed in advance. \u2022 The total elapsed journey time allowed for each train is T\u202f>\u202f0. We use a basic separation scheme with a single vector h = { h j } j = 0 n of prescribed intermediate times 0 < h 0 < h 1 < \u22ef < h n \u2212 2 < h n \u2212 1 = T < h n = T + \u0394 T . The latest allowed exit time from section ( x j , x j + 1 ) for the leading train is h \u2113 , j + 1 = h j for j = 0 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 and the earliest allowed entry time to section ( x j \u2212 1 , x j ) for the following train is h f , j \u2212 1 = h j + c j for j = 1 , \u22ef , n . The constant cj \u202f>\u202f0 is large enough to ensure that the back of the leading train has left the section ( x j , x j + 1 ) before the following train enters section ( x j \u2212 1 , x j ) . The time \u0394T\u202f>\u202f0 is the difference between the starting times for the two trains. \u2022 For convenience we will assume that c j = c for all j = 1 , \u2026 , n so that after the front of the leading train has passed a given signal location the additional time allowed to ensure that the entire train has passed that location will be the same for each signal location. In this case the elapsed time constraints t f ( x j \u2212 1 ) \u2212 t f ( 0 ) \u2265 h f , j \u2212 1 \u2212 h f , 0 = ( h j + c ) \u2212 ( h 1 + c ) = h j \u2212 h 1 on the following train do not depend on c. Thus the optimal speed profile v f = v f ( x ) for the following train does not depend on c. Hence the optimal speed profile for the following train can be found by simply assuming that c = 0 . For the remainder of the paper we will assume without loss of generality that c = 0 . \u2022 For the leading train we require t \u2113 ( 0 ) = 0 and t \u2113 ( X ) \u2264 T \u21d4 t \u2113 ( x n ) \u2212 t \u2113 ( 0 ) \u2264 h n \u2212 1 . The front of the leading train must pass location x j + 1 no later than time hj . We model this by specifying supplementary intermediate time constraints t \u2113 ( x j + 1 ) \u2212 t \u2113 ( 0 ) \u2264 h \u2113 , j + 1 \u21d4 t \u2113 ( x j + 1 ) \u2264 h j for each j = 0 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . \u2022 For the following train we require t f ( 0 ) = \u0394 T \u2265 h 1 + c = h \u2113 , 2 + c and t f ( X ) \u2264 T + \u0394 T \u21d4 t f ( x n ) \u2212 t f ( 0 ) \u2264 h n \u2212 1 . We also know that the front of the following train must not pass location x j \u2212 1 before the back of the leading train has passed location x j + 1 . We model this by specifying supplementary intermediate time constraints t f ( x j \u2212 1 ) \u2212 t f ( 0 ) \u2265 h j \u2212 h 2 \u21d4 t f ( x j \u2212 1 ) \u2265 h j + c for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . As noted earlier we can simplify these inequalities by setting c = 0 without disrupting the optimal speed profile. 1.6 Terminology Throughout the paper we use specific terminology to refer to particular aspects of the problem. We will summarize the terminology here. \u2022 Section clearance times is a collective term for both the latest allowed section exit times and the earliest allowed section entry times. \u2022 For the leading train the intermediate time constraint t \u2113(xj )\u202f\u2264\u202fh \u2113,j will be described as active if t \u2113 ( x j ) = h \u2113 , j and inactive if t \u2113(xj )\u202f<\u202fh \u2113,j . For the following train the intermediate time constraint tf (xj )\u202f\u2265\u202fh f,j will be described as active if t f ( x j ) = h f , j and inactive if tf (xj )\u202f>\u202fh f,j . \u2022 For each train an actively-timed segment is the union of one or more consecutive sections ( x p , x p + 1 ) , \u2026 , ( x q , x q + 1 ) where the time constraints at xp and x q + 1 are active but the time constraints at all intermediate signal locations xj for p\u202f<\u202fj\u202f\u2264\u202fq are inactive. The actively-timed segments for the leading train are not necessarily the same as the actively-timed segments for the following train. \u2022 The sequence { V j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 is decreasing if V 0 \u2265 \u22ef \u2265 V n \u2212 1 and strictly decreasing if V 0 > \u22ef > V n \u2212 1 . The sequence is increasing if V 0 \u2264 \u22ef \u2264 V n \u2212 1 and strictly increasing if V 0 < \u22ef < V n \u2212 1 . \u2022 A strategy of optimal type satisfies the necessary conditions for optimality but does not necessarily satisfy the imposed time constraints. A strategy of optimal type for the leading train is defined by a decreasing sequence { V \u2113 , j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 of optimal driving speeds where V \u2113,j denotes the optimal driving speed on the section ( x j , x j + 1 ) . A strategy of optimal type for the following train is defined by an increasing sequence { V f , j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 of optimal driving speeds where V f,j denotes the optimal driving speed on the section ( x j , x j + 1 ) . \u2022 A feasible strategy is one that satisfies the imposed time constraints. 1.7 Point-mass trains Howlett and Pudney (1995) showed that the motion of a train with known distributed mass could be modeled as the motion of a point-mass train using a modified track gradient profile. Although we model all trains in this paper as point-mass trains in order to calculate the speed profiles we can nevertheless assume for purposes of train separation that the trains have non-zero length. 1.8 An important note about the mathematical solutions The mathematical solutions to both the leading train problem and the following train problem are defined from the equations of motion by the respective speed profiles v \u2113 = v \u2113 ( x ) and v f = v f ( x ) and the respective elapsed journey times t \u2113 ( x ) \u2212 t \u2113 ( 0 ) and t f ( x ) \u2212 t f ( 0 ) expressed as functions of position. If we specify the intermediate time constraints in the form (1) t \u2113 ( x j ) \u2212 t \u2113 ( 0 ) \u2264 h \u2113 , j \u21d4 t \u2113 ( x j ) \u2264 h \u2113 , j for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 where 0 < h \u2113 , 1 < \u22ef < h \u2113 , n \u2212 1 < T are the clearance times for the leading train and in the form (2) t f ( x j ) \u2212 t f ( 0 ) \u2265 h f , j for each j = 0 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 where 0 < h f , 0 < \u22ef < h f , n \u2212 1 < T are the clearance times for the following train then the two problem formulations and their solutions are completely independent. For this reason the detailed theoretical solution to the leading train problem depends only on the specified clearance times for the leading train and does not depend in any way on the detailed solution to the following train problem. In similar fashion the detailed theoretical solution to the following train problem depends only on the specified clearance times for the following train and does not depend in any way on the detailed solution to the leading train problem. Nevertheless the whole point of the discussion is to relate the two sets of clearance times so that the following train cannot enter any particular section until the leading train has left the subsequent section. Thus we set the starting times as t \u2113 ( 0 ) = 0 and t f ( 0 ) = h f , 0 = \u0394 T \u2265 h \u2113 , 2 + c and choose sequences { h \u2113 , j } j = 0 n and { h f , j } j = 0 n so that (3) h f , j \u2212 1 \u2212 h f , 0 \u2265 h \u2113 , j + 1 \u2212 h \u2113 , 2 for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . Inequality (3) means that the following train must take at least as long to traverse the interval ( x 0 , x j \u2212 1 ) as the leading train takes to traverse the interval ( x 2 , x j + 1 ) . Now h f , j \u2212 1 \u2265 h \u2113 , j + 1 + h f , 0 \u2212 h \u2113 , 2 \u21d2 t f ( x j \u2212 1 ) \u2265 h \u2113 , j + 1 + c \u21d2 t f ( x j \u2212 1 ) \u2265 t \u2113 ( x j + 1 ) + c and so the back of the leading train must pass x j + 1 before the following train reaches x j \u2212 1 . If we choose \u0394 T = h \u2113 , 2 + c and h f , j \u2212 1 \u2212 h f , 0 = h \u2113 , j + 1 \u2212 h \u2113 , 2 in (3) then we have h f , j \u2212 1 = h \u2113 , j + 1 = h j for all j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 with h 0 = h \u2113 , 1 and h n = h f , n \u2212 1 . Thus, in this case, the intermediate clearance times are effectively defined by a single sequence { h j } j = 0 n . We will show that the optimal speed profile for the leading train is uniquely determined by a decreasing sequence of optimal driving speeds { V \u2113 , j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 where V \u2113,j is the optimal driving speed on section ( x j , x j + 1 ) for each j = 0 , 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . Provided xj is not on a steep uphill or steep downhill slope the optimal speed v \u2113 ( x j ) = U \u2113 , j at the location xj must satisfy V \u2113 , j \u2212 1 \u2265 U \u2113 , j \u2265 V \u2113 , j for each j = 0 , 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . Thus it is normally sufficient to choose c \u2265 L / V \u2113 , n \u2212 1 where V \u2113 , n \u2212 1 is the optimal driving speed for the leading train on the final interval ( x n \u2212 1 , x n ) and L is the length of the train. The speed profiles and the elapsed journey times for each train depend only on position. Adjustments to convert elapsed journey times to real times do not change the calculated values for the speeds v \u2113 = v \u2113 ( x ) and v f = v f ( x ) and elapsed journey times t \u2113 ( x ) \u2212 t \u2113 ( 0 ) = t \u2113 ( x ) and t f ( x ) \u2212 t f ( 0 ) = t f ( x ) \u2212 \u0394 T at location x. We will also show that the optimal speed profile for the following train is uniquely determined in a similar way by an increasing sequence of optimal driving speeds { V f , j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 where V f,j is the optimal driving speed on section ( x j , x j + 1 ) for each j = 0 , 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . If the signal locations are not on steep segments of track then the speed v f ( x j ) = U f , j at the location xj will satisfy V f , j \u2212 1 \u2264 U f , j \u2264 V f , j for each j = 0 , 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . 1.9 Contribution The main contribution of this paper is to establish a sound mathematical basis on which to develop a theory of energy-efficient driving strategies for two trains traveling on the same line in the same direction subject to imposed separation constraints that enable sensible driving and respect safe-operating conditions. This has not been an easy problem to formulate in tractable form. In retrospect the key idea has been finding a mathematical separation of the original problem into two separate and essentially independent problems\u2014one for the leading train and one for the following train\u2014that enable us to find characteristic forms for the strategies of optimal type. The separation is achieved by imposing a set of prescribed clearance times for each train on each section. In practice this simply means that the network operator would need to impose latest allowed exit times for the leading train and earliest allowed entry times for the following train on each timed section. We can now solve each problem separately by including the intermediate time constraints as penalty terms in the energy consumption cost functional and applying the Pontryagin principle. Because we have reduced the two-train separation problem to two mathematically separate single train problems the corresponding strategy of optimal type for each train can be regarded as a sequence of optimal single train strategies (Albrecht et al., 2015a; 2015b; Howlett, 2000; Khmelnitsky, 2000; Liu and Golovitcher, 2003) on each successive section. We show that the strategy of optimal type for each train is defined by an optimal driving speed on each actively-timed section. In order to establish this key idea our mathematical argument assumes that only the active constraints are considered in which case we can show that the sequence of optimal driving speeds is a strictly decreasing sequence for the leading train and a strictly increasing sequence for the following train. In practice, particularly from a notational point of view, it is often more convenient to retain all of the intermediate time-constraints\u2014both active and inactive. If the time constraint at a particular signal location is inactive for one of the trains then the optimal driving speed for that train does does not change at that particular signal location. Thus, with this convention, the respective sequences of optimal driving speeds will simply be increasing and decreasing rather than strictly increasing and strictly decreasing. We have also described a general procedure for calculating the optimal strategies for each train and have illustrated the process with several particular examples. 1.10 Separation constraints and optimal driving strategies\u2014the basic dilemma For the sake of notational and algebraic simplicity we assume that the two trains are identical but we hasten to add that this assumption is not necessary. If no intermediate clearance times are imposed it is known (Albrecht et al., 2013a) that the optimal strategy for the entire journey is completely defined by a single parameter\u2014the optimal driving speed. For track with no steep segments the optimal strategy will consist of an initial phase of maximum acceleration, an intermediate phase of speedhold with partial acceleration at the optimal driving speed, a phase of coast and a final phase of maximum brake. For track with steep segments the optimal strategy may include additional intermediate phases of maximum acceleration to traverse steep uphill segments and additional phases of coast or speedhold with partial braking at the optimal regenerative braking speed to traverse steep downhill segments. The optimal speed profile is completely determined by the journey time and the optimal driving speed. For convenience we will refer to this strategy as the unrestricted optimal strategy. Can we use this strategy or related strategies to find feasible intermediate clearance times? Suppose the leading train uses the unrestricted optimal strategy with speed profile v 0 = v 0 ( x ) and starts at time h 0 , 0 = 0 . Then we can calculate the time t \u2113 ( x j ) = \u222b 0 x j d x v 0 ( x ) = h 0 , j when the leading train reaches the location xj for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n . We have written h 0,j to denote the elapsed time taken for a train to travel from signal location x 0 = 0 to signal location xj using the unrestricted optimal strategy. If the following train starts at time h f , 0 = \u0394 T and also uses the unrestricted optimal strategy then we can calculate the time t f ( x j ) = h f , 0 + \u222b 0 x j d x v 0 ( x ) = \u0394 T + h 0 , j when the following train reaches xj for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n . However we require t f ( x j \u2212 1 ) \u2265 t \u2113 ( x j + 1 ) because the following train cannot enter ( x j \u2212 1 , x j ) until the leading train has left ( x j , x j + 1 ) . Thus we must have h 0 , j \u2212 1 + \u0394 T \u2265 h 0 , j + 1 for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . If we want both trains to use the unrestricted optimal strategy then we must choose \u0394 T \u2265 \u0394 T 0 = max j = 1 n \u2212 1 [ h 0 , j + 1 \u2212 h 0 , j \u2212 1 ] . Let us suppose \u0394T\u202f<\u202f\u0394T 0 and there is some location xs where h 0 , s \u2212 1 + \u0394 T < h 0 , s + 1 . This means that the following train enters ( x s \u2212 1 , x s ) before the leading train has left ( x s , x s + 1 ) . Thus it is no longer possible for both trains to use the unrestricted optimal strategy. We could consider the possibility that only one of the trains uses the unrestricted optimal strategy. If the leading train uses the unrestricted optimal strategy then the following train would need to use a strategy with speed profile v f = v f ( x ) such that t f ( x j \u2212 1 ) = \u0394 T + \u222b 0 x j \u2212 1 d x v f ( x ) \u2265 h 0 , j + 1 but this would fail if t \u2113 ( x 2 ) = h 0 , 2 > \u0394 T . If the following train uses the unrestricted optimal strategy then the leading train would need to use a strategy with speed profile v \u2113 = v \u2113 ( x ) such that t \u2113 ( x j + 1 ) = \u222b 0 x j + 1 d x v \u2113 ( x ) \u2264 h 0 , j \u2212 1 + \u0394 T but this would fail if t f ( x n \u2212 1 ) = h 0 , n \u2212 1 + \u0394 T < T . This means that if \u0394T is small with \u0394T\u202f<\u202f\u0394T 0 then we may need to find bounds for the specified intermediate clearance times by considering reference strategies for the leading train that satisfy the overall time constraint but are faster early on in the journey than the unrestricted optimal strategy and by considering reference strategies for the following train that satisfy the overall time constraint but are slower early on in the journey than the unrestricted optimal strategy. One useful reference strategy is the minimum-time strategy which uses only two control phases\u2014maximum acceleration and maximum brake. 2 Two examples to illustrate the problem We will illustrate the general problem with two examples using realistic values for the gradient accelerations and other train parameters. In practice gradient accelerations are tabulated by network operators as piecewise constant functions using intervals that are typically of order 100\u202fm in length. In these two examples we use a much simplified gradient profile but point out that this is merely a device to avoid tedious but routine numerical calculations. We assume that regenerative braking is not used. The examples use basically the same data. In Example 1 we simply find the optimal unrestricted strategy for the entire trip and calculate the required delay for the following train to ensure adequate separation of the two trains if both trains wish to use the unrestricted optimal strategy. In Example 2 we will show how to calculate feasible bounds for the section clearance times required to ensure adequate separation in Example 1. We will return to these examples later and show that even when it is not possible for both trains to use the unrestricted optimal strategy it may still be possible to find feasible prescribed intermediate clearance times and hence ultimately to find an optimal solution. The track is classified as steep uphill when the optimal driving speed V satisfies V\u202f>\u202fV ma,up. The limiting speed v = V c , down under coast on the downhill segment when x\u202f\u2208\u202f(35320, 47660) is obtained by solving the equation \u2212 r ( v ) + 0.0194 = 0 to give V c,down\u202f\u2248\u202f25.033 ms \u2212 1 . The track is classified as steep downhill if the optimal driving speed V satisfies V\u202f<\u202fV c,down. As we change the optimal driving speed V the form of the optimal strategy changes too. There is no steep uphill segment of track for V\u202f<\u202fV ma,up and there is no steep downhill segment of track for V\u202f>\u202fV c,down. Suppose the total allowed journey time is T = 3840 s. On track with piecewise constant gradient it is possible to calculate position and time as functions of speed using elementary integrals with analytic integrands (Howlett, 2016). It is also known (Albrecht et al., 2015a; 2015b; Howlett et al., 2009; Liu and Golovitcher, 2003; Vu, 2006) that the adjoint variables can be expressed as algebraic functions of the speed. We have used these functions to calculate the optimal switching points. We used problem-specific Matlab algorithms and standard Matlab packages such as the numerical integration routine quadgk for all of our calculations. The unrestricted optimal strategy is shown in Fig. 1 . It is uniquely determined by the optimal driving speed V\u202f\u2248\u202f19.912. With this value of V there is no steep uphill segment but a phase of coast is required to traverse the steep downhill segment (35320, 47660). Thus the optimal strategy is maximum acceleration, speedhold at the optimal driving speed, coast, speedhold at the optimal driving speed, coast and maximum brake. The control changes occur at the points \u03be = ( \u03be 1 , \u2026 , \u03be 5 ) \u2248 ( 3352 , 34074 , 49020 , 56926 , 69048 ) and the calculated times at the signal locations are denoted by t = ( t 0 , \u2026 , t 14 ) and given by t \u2248 ( 0 , 427 , 829 , 980 , 1138 , 1289 , 1443 , 1646 , 1851 , 2118 , 2468 , 2618 , 2893 , 3217 , 3840 ) . The speeds at the top and bottom of the steep decline during the coast phase are U 8\u202f\u2248\u202f18.906 and U 10\u202f\u2248\u202f20.994, respectively, and the speed at which braking begins is U = \u03c8 ( V ) / \u03c6 \u2032 ( V ) \u2248 8.5065 . The cost for any feasible solution to the two-train separation problem must be at least double this amount. Suppose we wish to run two successive identical trains over this journey with the leading train starting at time 0 and finishing at time T = 3840 and the following train starting at time \u0394 T = 720 and finishing at time T + \u0394 T = 4560 . If we assume that the leading train uses the unrestricted optimal strategy then the signal times for the leading train will be t \u2113 = t . If the following train also uses the unrestricted optimal strategy then the signal times for the following train are t f = t + \u0394 T \u00d7 1 which gives t f \u2248 ( 720 , 1147 , 1549 , 1700 , 1858 , 2009 , 2163 , 2366 , 2571 , 2838 , 3188 , 3338 , 3613 , 3937 , 4560 ) . The separation constraints require t f ( x j \u2212 1 ) \u2265 t \u2113 ( x j + 1 ) for all j = 1 , \u2026 , 13 . This is clearly not the case. Thus it is not possible for both trains to use the unrestricted optimal strategy with \u0394 T = 720 . However if we set \u0394 T \u2265 \u0394 T 0 = max j = 1 13 [ t \u2113 , j + 1 \u2212 t \u2113 , j \u2212 1 ] \u2248 947 then both trains can use the unrestricted optimal strategy. The total cost in this case is J\u202f\u2248\u202f2050.70. \u25a1 In Example 1 we showed that both trains can use the unrestricted optimal strategy only if (8) \u0394 T \u2265 max j = 1 n \u2212 1 [ t \u2113 , j + 1 \u2212 t \u2113 , j \u2212 1 ] . The same argument applies to any feasible strategy. If the leading train uses a particular strategy with signal times t \u2113 then the following train can use the same strategy only if (8) is satisfied. Of course it is not necessary for both trains to use an identical strategy. The whole point of this paper is to show firstly that it may be possible to find feasible strategies when the unrestricted optimal strategy does not satisfy (8) and secondly to show that in such circumstances we can also find optimal strategies that minimize the total tractive energy consumption. Although each strategy may differ from the unrestricted optimal strategy the strategy must still satisfy basic optimality requirements. Indeed it is necessarily true for both trains that any strategy of optimal type must also be optimal on each section subject to the values determined for the initial and final speeds on that section and the time taken to traverse the section. To find bounds on the prescribed clearance times we must find a strategy for the leading train that travels as quickly as possible for the first part of the journey and a strategy for the following train that travels as slowly as possible for the first part of the journey. Our second example shows how we might determine reasonable bounds for the prescribed intermediate times in Example 1. Example 2 Suppose we wish to find suitable bounds for the prescribed intermediate clearance times in Example 1. Ironically we can find both the upper and lower bounds using essentially the same strategy\u2014the minimum-time strategy. The time allowed for each train to complete the journey is T = 3840 s and the initial delay for the following train is \u0394 T = 720 s. For the leading train we use the speed profile v = v min ( x ) for the minimum-time strategy beginning at t = 0 and finishing at t = T min < T . For the following train we use the same speed profile v = v min ( x ) for the minimum-time strategy starting at t = T + \u0394 T \u2212 T min > \u0394 T and finishing at t = T + \u0394 T . To avoid ridiculously high speeds we will impose a speed limit V L = 32 ms \u2212 1 . Now the minimum-time strategy will be maximum acceleration, speedhold at the speed limit VL and maximum brake. We used a Matlab program to find the minimum-time strategy subject to the constraint v(x)\u202f\u2264\u202fVL (x) for all x\u202f\u2208\u202f[0, X]. The recent review by Scheepmaker et al. (2017) provides a comprehensive summary of the work on optimal driving strategies. From a wider perspective the optimal driving strategy for each individual train in the two-train separation problem depends on the maximum allowed journey time and on the prescribed intermediate section clearance times for the required separation. There is no published theoretical solution to this problem although there have been numerous papers proposing numerical solutions to related scheduling problems. Scheepmaker et al. (2017) also review the work on development of energy-efficient network timetables. A broadly-based review of automatic train operation and related technologies can be found in the paper by Yin et al. (2017). 3.1 Classical methods for the optimal control of a single train Our formulation of the two-train separation problem and our application of the Pontryagin principle is new but is closely related to a substantial body of published work that uses the Pontryagin principle and related techniques of constrained optimization to find necessary conditions on optimal driving strategies for a single train. We will not attempt to review this well-established work here. For details of the underlying theory we refer readers to the key papers on continuous control by Albrecht et al. (2013a, 2015a, 2015b), Baranov et al. (2011), Howlett (2000, 2016), Howlett et al. (2009), Khmelnitsky (2000) and Liu and Golovitcher (2003). The frequently-cited papers on discrete control (Cheng and Howlett, 1992; Howlett and Cheng, 1993; Howlett et al., 1994; Howlett, 1996; Howlett and Leizarowitz, 2001) and the early papers on continuous control (Asnis et al., 1985; Howlett, 1990; Ichikawa, 1968; Kokotovic and Singh, 1972; Milroy, 1981; Strobel and Horn, 1973) contain important insights but for the most part are not directly related to this paper. Other significant studies in the Russian literature (Baranov et al., 1990; Golovitcher, 1986a; 1986b; 1989b; 1989a; Isayev, 1995) may be difficult to obtain and in most cases the relevant results can be extracted from more recent papers. A recent paper by Lin et al. (2017) looks at optimal control of freight trains on long-distance steep downhill grades. This is an important practical application. The paper is theoretically sound and includes a very readable presentation of the relevant Pontryagin analysis. Finally it is important to note that these classical strategies have been implemented in real-time on very fast trains (Albrecht et al., 2015b; Technology, 0000) and that updated strategies can be recalculated on-board in a matter of a few seconds (Albrecht et al., 2015b). 3.2 Hybrid methods for the control of one or more trains Some authors have proposed the use of search techniques in order to find or modify strategies of optimal type. A common rationale for the use of hybrid methods is a desire to broaden the objective function to include journey times, passenger satisfaction and network timing constraints as well as energy efficiency. A mistaken belief that the classical conditions for optimal switching cannot be computed in real-time is no longer tenable. See Albrecht et al. (2015b, Section 9) for an application of the Energymiser system (Technology, 0000) to the TGV train in France. Hybrid methods are mostly not directly related to the mathematics used in this paper. Although these methods have used legitimate numerical calculations to find timetables that allow or encourage energy-efficient driving strategies and respect safe separation constraints they have not been used\u2014and cannot be used\u2014to solve the theoretical problem posed in this paper. ShangGuan et al. (2016) use differential evolution and simulated annealing with a Pareto objective to find and update de facto strategies of optimal type in order to lower energy consumption and arrive on time. The proposed strategies were simulated using data for a very fast train on the Wuhan\u2013Guangzhou line in China. The line has no steep grades and drivers currently use a maximum acceleration-speedhold-maximum brake strategy. The maximum acceleration-speedhold-coast-maximum brake strategies proposed in the paper show decreased energy consumption and improved time-keeping in simulation. The authors argued that introduction of a coast phase was a significant factor in reducing energy consumption and they also noted that progressive adjustments to the key switching points was a critical ingredient in improving on-time arrival. Wang and Goverde (2016) study the two-train separation problem using a so-called green wave operating policy. The overall journey includes scheduled stops at some intermediate stations with mandatory time-windows at each station and with additional intermediate speed and time-windows at signal locations between stations. In normal operation\u2014when there are no disruptions to the planned timetable\u2014the time-windows for arrival and departure at each station are reduced to specific times. When the timetable is disrupted by unplanned events the trains are encouraged to gradually recover the original schedule by allowing expanded speed and time-windows at the various intermediate stations and signal locations. The objective function finds a compromise between energy-efficiency and passenger comfort. Yang et al. (2018) use passenger demand to determine headway and cycle times on a busy metropolitan line and then design energy-efficient speed profiles using maximum acceleration, coasting and maximum braking. The speed profiles respect imposed speed limits but are designed using a grossly simplified model of the train dynamics that assumes constant rates of acceleration and braking. Ye and Liu (2016) formulate a multi-train optimal control problem for trains travelling in the same direction on the same line. They assume a predetermined time order for arrivals and departures at all stations and meeting locations. The problem is formulated using a standard model for the train dynamics and is discretized using a Radau pseudospectral method with Legendre\u2013Gauss\u2013Radau collocation points and then solved as a nonlinear programming problem to optimize arrival and departure times and all running and dwell times. The methods were implemented successfully on two relatively small problems but the authors warn that the complexity will increase when the number of trains or the number of stations increases. Zhou et al. (2017) use dynamic programming to find energy-efficient schedules and optimized speed profiles for a large number of trains on a high-speed rail corridor with power supply and line capacity constraints. They construct a three-dimensional space-time-speed grid network and systematically integrate problems of macroscopic train timetabling and microscopic train trajectory calculations. A case study of the Beijing\u2013Shanghai high-speed rail corridor is used in simulations to demonstrate the effectiveness and computational efficiency of the proposed algorithms. 3.3 Design of energy-efficient network timetables In this paper we have assumed that total journey times are specified by an imposed timetable and that typical journeys may include intermediate signal locations where trains are not expected to stop but where there are prescribed time-windows within which the train is expected to pass the signal location. There are many different methods that have been used for timetable design but the design methods per se are not directly relevant to the optimal driving strategies devised here. As long as the journey times and the intermediate clearance times are feasible our proposed strategies can be implemented. These driving strategies will minimize tractive energy consumption subject to the specified journey times and prescribed intermediate clearance times even if the estimated section running-times are based on grossly simplified speed profiles or empirical cost\u2013time curves. There is a vast body of work on the design and operation of train timetables on large rail networks. The problems are often extremely complex and optimal solutions are normally not possible. However there are many methods that have been used to find feasible timetables. The most successful methods use mathematical programming or modern search techniques. For a more detailed discussion we cite two established papers by Caprara et al. (2002) and Cordeaux et al. (1998) and some more recent work on real-time scheduling and conflict resolution by D\u2019Ariano et al. (2007). Albrecht et al. (2013b) claim that the use of time-windows instead of target times at minor stops and junctions can decrease energy consumption. They warn that target windows should only be applied if slight delays for an individual train do not have immediate consequences for surrounding trains and claim that an algorithm described by Albrecht and Oettich (2002) can be easily extended to allow time-windows. Jaekel and Albrecht (2013) extended these ideas to define train path envelopes to limit the search space for energy-efficient speed profiles that maintain safe separation from adjacent trains. Wang and Goverde (2017) used a similar philosophy when they proposed a multi-train trajectory optimization method that finds optimal meeting locations, arrival and departure times, and speed trajectories for multiple trains within a designated set of feasible time and speed windows. In this regard we note that Wang et al. (2014) used a pseudo-spectral method and mixed integer linear programming to find energy-efficient trajectories for a fleet of trains separated by moving-block signalling. The results are applied to a particular example with only two trains\u2014a leading train and a following train\u2014but the authors claim that the methods extend easily to more than two trains. The incorporation of energy-efficient speed control for individual trains is now recognized as an important component in the design and operation of effective timetables (Albrecht et al., 2013b; Corman et al., 2012) but the times allocated for individual journeys are often a secondary consideration and use simplified speed profiles to estimate sectional costs as a function of sectional running times. Su et al. (2013, 2014) used a rudimentary model of the train dynamics to estimate potential energy savings on each section of track subject to additional running-time supplements that allow energy-efficient driving strategies. Although the model assumptions of constant tractive and braking forces and constant resistance are unrealistic the model does identify key locations where running-time supplements deliver the most significant energy savings. By finding an optimal distribution of the running-time supplements their simulations of a typical Beijing metro line showed significant potential reductions in total tractive energy consumption. An idea that has gained recent currency with electric trains is to construct a timetable that enables energy recovered from regenerative braking on one train to be used as driving power for another train. This requires the trains concerned to be connected to the same electrical substation at the time of the proposed energy transfer. Li and Lo (2014a) used a genetic algorithm to synchronize the accelerating and braking actions of trains while imposing an elementary optimal driving strategy to control tractive energy consumption on each individual journey. In a subsequent paper Li and Lo (2014b) formulated an integrated energy-efficient timetable and speed profile optimization model which was solved approximately by transforming to a simplified convex optimization problem. A recent paper by Liu and Zhao (2017) used heuristic methods to find schedules that maximize transfer of regenerated energy from arriving trains to departing trains on an urban subway line. A similar problem was solved approximately in Gupta et al. (2016) using a two-stage process analogous to the one used by Li and Lo (2014a,b). In the first stage the optimal running time for each train on each section was determined subject to the usual trip-time, dwell-time, headway and connection constraints. In the second stage the start and finish times for each journey were adjusted without changing the total journey time or the section running times. The aim was to synchronize, as far as possible, the arrivals and departures of co-located trains. A crucial part in the first stage of this process was the use of a characteristic cost\u2013time curve for each train on each section of track. The proposed solution in Gupta et al. (2016) used a linear approximation to an empirical cost\u2013time curve but did not consider a known key formula (Howlett, 2016; Liu and Golovitcher, 2003) for the slope of the cost\u2013time curve. Nor did it consider auxiliary formul\u00e6 (Howlett, 2016; Isayev, 1995) which show that the optimal distribution of sectional running times occurs when the optimal driving speeds on each section are equal. Since these formulae do not require detailed knowledge of the optimal strategy they could possibly be used to improve the initial estimation of journey costs. Yang et al. (2013, 2014, 2016) used similar ideas to find potential improvements in network operations by improved coordination of arrival and departure times. They concluded that there were potential cost savings through energy transfers between arriving and departing trains and likely benefits from improved customer satisfaction with reduced passenger transfer times. 4 The basic train model Let x\u202f\u2208\u202f[0, X] denote the position of the train. We wish to find a control function u = ( u a , u b ) where u a = u a ( x ) is the driving control and u b = u b ( x ) is the braking control that will minimize the tractive energy required to drive the train. The journey cost is given by (9) J ( u ) = \u222b 0 X [ u a \u2212 \u03c1 u b ] d x where \u03c1\u202f\u2208\u202f[0, 1) is the regeneration efficiency. We assume that (10) 0 \u2264 u a \u2264 H ( v ) and 0 \u2264 u b \u2264 K ( v ) where v = v ( x ) is the speed of the train and H(v)\u202f>\u202f0 and K(v)\u202f>\u202f0 are the respective upper bounds on the driving and braking controls. The functions for speed v = v ( x ) and time t = t ( x ) corresponding to the selected controls are defined by the equations of motion (11) d v d x = u a \u2212 u b \u2212 r ( v ) + g ( x ) v (12) d t d x = 1 v , and must satisfy the speed constraints (13) v ( 0 ) = v ( X ) = 0 and the overall time constraint (14) t ( X ) \u2212 t ( 0 ) = \u222b 0 X 1 v ( x ) d x \u2264 T . Henceforth we will assume that additional timing constraints are imposed on each train at the signal locations 0 = x 0 < x 1 < \u22ef < x n \u2212 1 < x n = X . For the sake of simplicity we will also assume there are no speed limits. If speed limits are imposed the basic principles of the underlying arguments remain the same. Speed limits are discussed in Section 13.2. 5 The leading train problem The formulation and solution of the leading train problem depends only on the specified clearance times for the leading train and does not depend on a detailed knowledge of the solution to the corresponding following train problem. Thus, for algebraic convenience throughout this section where there is no confusion, we will normally omit specific notational reference to the leading train. The leading train starts at time t = 0 . We wish to find a control function u = u \u2113 which minimizes (9) subject to (10) and determine the corresponding speed and time functions v = v \u2113 and t = t \u2113 satisfying (11)\u2013(14) and the additional intermediate time constraints (15) t ( x j ) \u2212 t ( 0 ) = \u222b 0 x j 1 v ( x ) d x \u2264 h \u2113 , j \u2212 h \u2113 , 0 = h \u2113 , j where 0 < h \u2113 , 1 < \u22ef < h \u2113 , n \u2212 1 < T and where we define h \u2113 , n = T so that (14) becomes a special case of (15) with j = n . The inequalities (15) mean that the leading train must pass the location xj at or before time h \u2113,j . We follow the approach used by Howlett and Leizarowitz (2001) and by Liu and Golovitcher (2003) on the classic single train problem and include the times taken to reach the prescribed signal locations as penalty terms in the cost function. We assume that all time constraints are active so that the unknown constant weight factors are strictly positive. Mathematically this means that inactive time constraints are simply omitted from the model. In practice constant speed strategies on selected sub-journeys can often be used to provisionally identify inactive constraints. If the associated optimal solution violates one of the omitted constraints then that constraint must be reinstated and a new solution computed. To maximize H w with respect to ua and ub subject to the constraints 0\u202f\u2264\u202fua \u202f\u2264\u202fH(v) and 0\u202f\u2264\u202fub \u202f\u2264\u202fK(v) we apply the Karush\u2013Kuhn\u2013Tucker conditions (22) \u2202 L w \u2202 u a = 0 \u21d4 \u03bc v \u2212 1 \u2212 \u03b1 1 + \u03b1 2 = 0 and \u2202 L w \u2202 u b = 0 \u21d4 \u2212 \u03bc v + \u03c1 \u2212 \u03b2 1 + \u03b2 2 = 0 and the complementary slackness conditions (23) \u03b1 1 ( H ( v ) \u2212 u a ) = 0 , \u03b1 2 u a = 0 , \u03b2 1 ( K ( v ) \u2212 u b ) = 0 and \u03b2 2 u b = 0 . The optimal control modes are essentially an embellishment of those for the classic single train problem (Albrecht et al., 2013a; 2015a; Howlett, 2000; Howlett et al., 2009; Khmelnitsky, 2000; Liu and Golovitcher, 2003). They can be identified as follows. Mode 1: \u03bc\u202f\u2208\u202f(v, \u221e). We have \u03b1 1 \u2212 \u03b1 2 = \u03bc / v \u2212 1 > 0 . Hence \u03b1 1 > 0 \u21d2 u a = H ( v ) > 0 \u21d2 \u03b1 2 = 0 \u21d2 \u03b1 1 = \u03bc / v \u2212 1 . We also have \u03b2 2 \u2212 \u03b2 1 = \u03bc / v \u2212 \u03c1 > 0 . Hence \u03b2 2 > 0 \u21d2 u b = 0 \u21d2 \u03b2 1 = 0 \u21d2 \u03b2 2 = \u03bc / v \u2212 \u03c1 . This is maximum acceleration. Mode 2: \u03bc = v . We have \u03b1 1 \u2212 \u03b1 2 = 0 . Hence \u03b1 1 = \u03b1 2 = 0 \u21d2 u a \u2208 [ 0 , H ( v ) ] . On the other hand \u03b2 2 \u2212 \u03b2 1 = 1 \u2212 \u03c1 > 0 . Hence \u03b2 2 > 0 \u21d2 u b = 0 \u21d2 \u03b2 1 = 0 \u21d2 \u03b2 2 = 1 \u2212 \u03c1 . This is partial acceleration. Mode 3: \u03bc\u202f\u2208\u202f(\u03c1 v, v). We have \u03b1 2 \u2212 \u03b1 1 = 1 \u2212 \u03bc / v > 0 . Hence \u03b1 2 > 0 \u21d2 u a = 0 \u21d2 \u03b1 1 = 0 \u21d2 \u03b1 2 = 1 \u2212 \u03bc / v . At the same time we have \u03b2 2 \u2212 \u03b2 1 = \u03bc / v \u2212 \u03c1 > 0 . Therefore \u03b2 2 > 0 \u21d2 u b = 0 \u21d2 \u03b2 1 = 0 \u21d2 \u03b2 2 = \u03bc / v \u2212 \u03c1 . This is coast. Mode 4: \u03bc = \u03c1 v . We have \u03b1 2 \u2212 \u03b1 1 = 1 \u2212 \u03c1 > 0 . Hence \u03b1 2 > 0 \u21d2 u a = 0 \u21d2 \u03b1 1 = 0 \u21d2 \u03b1 2 = 1 \u2212 \u03c1 . At the same time we have \u03b2 2 \u2212 \u03b2 1 = 0 . Therefore \u03b2 1 = \u03b2 2 = 0 and ub \u202f\u2208\u202f[0, K(v)]. This is partial brake. Mode 5: \u03bc \u2208 ( \u2212 \u221e , \u03c1 v ) . We have \u03b1 2 \u2212 \u03b1 1 = 1 \u2212 \u03bc / v > 1 \u2212 \u03c1 > 0 . Hence \u03b1 2 > 0 \u21d2 u a = 0 \u21d2 \u03b1 1 = 0 \u21d2 \u03b1 2 = 1 \u2212 \u03bc / v . At the same time we have \u03b2 1 \u2212 \u03b2 2 = \u03c1 \u2212 \u03bc / v > 0 . Therefore \u03b2 1 > 0 \u21d2 u b = K ( v ) \u21d2 \u03b2 2 = 0 \u21d2 \u03b2 1 = \u03c1 \u2212 \u03bc / v . This is maximum brake. Modes 1, 3 and 5 are regular control modes while Modes 2 and 4 are singular control modes. 6 The following train problem The formulation and solution of the following train problem depends only on the specified clearance times for the following train and does not depend on a detailed knowledge of the solution to the corresponding leading train problem. Thus, for algebraic convenience throughout this section, we will often omit specific notational reference to the following train. The following train starts at time t = \u0394 T = h f , 0 . We wish to find a control function u = u f which minimizes (9) subject to (10) and corresponding speed and time functions v = v f and t = t f satisfying (11)\u2013(14) and the additional time constraints (24) t ( x j ) \u2212 t ( 0 ) = \u222b 0 x j 1 v ( x ) d x \u2265 h f , j where \u0394 T = h f , 0 < h f , 1 < \u22ef < h f , n \u2212 1 < T + \u0394 T and where we define h f , n = T + \u0394 T . The inequalities (24) mean that the following train cannot enter the section ( x j , x j + 1 ) until time h f , j + \u0394 T . We have formulated the problem in such a way that there is no direct mathematical connection to the leading train problem. Hence the following train problem can be solved with no specific knowledge of the solution to the leading train problem. Once again we include the times taken to reach the prescribed signal locations as penalty terms in the cost function and assume that all time constraints are active. Thus the unknown weight factors are strictly positive. Note that \u03bb 0 > \u03bb 1 > \u22ef > \u03bb n \u2212 1 > 0 . To maximize H w with respect to the controls ua and ub at each point subject to the control constraints 0\u202f\u2264\u202fua \u202f\u2264\u202fH(v) and 0\u202f\u2264\u202fub \u202f\u2264\u202fK(v) we define the associated Lagrangian L w = L f , w given formally by (20) and use the general relationship d \u03bc / d x = \u2212 \u2202 L w / \u2202 v to find the adjoint differential equation (28) d \u03bc d x = \u2212 \u2211 i = 0 n \u2212 1 ( \u03bb \u2212 \u03bb i ) \u03c7 ( x i , x i + 1 ) v 2 + \u03bc r \u2032 ( v ) v + \u03bc ( u a \u2212 u b \u2212 r ( v ) + g ) v 2 \u2212 \u03b1 1 H \u2032 ( v ) \u2212 \u03b2 1 K \u2032 ( v ) . To complete the task we apply the Karush\u2013Kuhn\u2013Tucker conditions and the complementary slackness conditions to find necessary conditions for the optimal controls. The optimal control modes are precisely the same as they were for the leading train so the detailed explanations are omitted. However we will show that the sequence of optimal controls for the following train is not the same as it is for the leading train. 7 The singular optimal control modes for the leading train Let us consider what happens when a singular partial acceleration phase for the leading train is maintained over a non-trivial interval. We also consider in more detail what happens when a singular partial brake phase is maintained over a non-trivial interval. Since \u03bc = \u03c1 v it follows from (11) and (28) that (32) d \u03bc d x = \u03c1 d v d x \u21d4 \u2212 \u2211 i = 0 n \u2212 1 ( \u03bb \u2212 \u03bb i ) \u03c7 ( x i , x i + 1 ) v 2 + \u03c1 r \u2032 ( v ) = 0 \u21d4 \u03c1 \u03c8 ( v ) = \u2211 i = 0 n \u2212 1 ( \u03bb \u2212 \u03bb i ) \u03c7 ( x i , x i + 1 ) . Hence the optimal partial brake phase is also a speedhold phase with holdspeed Wi on the interval ( x i , x i + 1 ) . We say that W i = W f , i is the optimal regenerative braking speed on the interval ( x i , x i + 1 ) . Once again the optimal speeds Vi and Wi on ( x i , x i + 1 ) for partial acceleration and partial brake respectively are related by the formula W i = \u03c8 \u2212 1 [ \u03c8 ( V i ) / \u03c1 ] . 9 The basic structure of the optimal strategies On level track the two-train separation problem has recently been solved (Albrecht et al., 2015c; 2015d; 2015e). The optimal strategies for both trains on level track involve successive segments of speedhold at the optimal driving speed separated by segments of regular control to transition from one speedhold phase to the next. On tracks that are not level but have no steep segments one might reasonably expect the optimal strategies to retain this same general form. This is indeed the case. In this section we will simply outline the basic form of the optimal strategies on track with no steep segments. The optimal strategy for the leading train is defined by a decreasing sequence of optimal driving speeds { V \u2113 , j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 . The optimal strategy begins with maximum acceleration to reach the optimal driving speed V \u2113,0 at p \u2113,0\u202f\u2208\u202f(x 0,x 1), follows with alternate phases of speedhold at speed V \u2113 , j \u2212 1 from p \u2113 , j \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x j \u2212 1 , x j ) to q \u2113 , j \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x j \u2212 1 , x j ) and coast from speed V \u2113 , j \u2212 1 at q \u2113 , j \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x j \u2212 1 , x j ) to speed V \u2113,j at p \u2113 , j \u2208 ( x j , x j + 1 ) for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 and finishes with phases of speedhold at speed V \u2113 , n \u2212 1 from p \u2113 , n \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x n \u2212 1 , x n ) to q \u2113 , n \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x n \u2212 1 , x n ) , coast from speed V \u2113 , n \u2212 1 at q \u2113 , n \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x n \u2212 1 , x n ) to speed U \u2113,n at r \u2113 , n \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x n \u2212 1 , x n ) and maximum brake from speed U \u2113,n at r \u2113 , n \u2212 1 to speed 0 at xn . The precise positions of the coast phases are determined by the conditions for optimal switching. The optimal strategy for the following train is defined by an increasing sequence of optimal driving speeds { V f , j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 . The optimal strategy begins with maximum acceleration to reach the optimal driving speed V f,0 at p f,0\u202f\u2208\u202f(x 0, x 1), follows with alternate phases of speedhold at speed V f , j \u2212 1 from p f , j \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x j \u2212 1 , x j ) to q f , j \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x j \u2212 1 , x j ) and maximum acceleration from speed V f , j \u2212 1 at q f , j \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x j \u2212 1 , x j ) to speed V f,j at p f , j \u2208 ( x j , x j + 1 ) for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 and finishes with phases of speedhold at speed V f , n \u2212 1 from p f , n \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x n \u2212 1 , x n ) to q f , n \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x n \u2212 1 , x n ) , coast from speed V f , n \u2212 1 at q f , n \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x n \u2212 1 , x n ) to speed U f,n at r f , n \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x n \u2212 1 , x n ) and maximum brake from speed U f,n at r f , n \u2212 1 to speed 0 at xn . The precise positions of the maximum acceleration phases are determined by the conditions for optimal switching. On level track it has been shown (Albrecht et al., 2015c; 2015d; 2015e) for both trains that the position of the transition phase of regular control from speed V j \u2212 1 at q j \u2212 1 to speed Vj at pj is determined the formula U j = \u03c8 ( V j \u2212 1 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V j ) \u03c6 \u2032 ( V j \u2212 1 ) \u2212 \u03c6 \u2032 ( V j ) where U j = v ( x j ) is the speed at the intervening signal location for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . This formula is derived by using the continuity of the adjoint variable at x = x j . On the final leg of the journey for the following train it is often the case that the optimal strategy is maximum acceleration, coast and maximum brake with no phase of speedhold at the optimal driving speed. In this case the optimal driving speed V n \u2212 1 is higher than the switching speed V n \u2212 1 * and the formula for the speed at x n \u2212 1 must be modified to give U n \u2212 1 = \u03c8 ( V n \u2212 1 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V n \u2212 2 ) [ \u03c6 \u2032 ( V n \u2212 1 * ) \u2212 \u03c6 \u2032 ( V n \u2212 2 ) ] + [ \u03c8 ( V n \u2212 1 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V n \u2212 1 * ) ] / V n \u2212 1 * . This formula is used in Examples 3 and 4. The speed at which braking begins is given by U n = \u03c8 ( V n \u2212 1 ) \u03c6 \u2032 ( V n \u2212 1 ) . These formulae do not apply on non-level track. The basic optimal strategy for the classic single train control problem must be interrupted by phases of regular control to traverse steep segments of track. The same is true for the basic optimal strategies described here. 10 An example with non-identical trains and intermediate stops In Example 3 our methods will be applied to find optimal strategies for journeys where both the leading train and following train are required to make intermediate stops. The track is level but the trains are not identical. We consider several different aspects of the problem. In the first place we find unrestricted optimal strategies for each train subject only to an overall time constraint and with no requirement to stop at an intermediate station. In the second place we find optimal strategies for each train when there is one stopping point and the journey is taken in two unrestricted parts. We assume the same overall journey time but require the leading train to stop at one station and the following train to stop at another. We also nominate specific times for each part of the journey. By comparing the unrestricted journeys with and without stopping we can show how much additional cost is incurred by making the trains stop at an intermediate point. We can also show how much delay is required for the following train if we want both trains to use these unrestricted optimal strategies but remain adequately separated at all times. Finally, in the third place, we show that it is possible to maintain the required separation but reduce the delay if we prescribe suitable intermediate signal clearance times and allow the trains to use the special restricted optimal strategies proposed in this paper. Example 3 We assume the track is level. Position is measured in metres and time is measured in seconds. The equally-spaced signal locations are denoted by x = ( x 0 , \u2026 , x 10 ) where x 0 = 0 , x 1 = 5000 , \u2026 , x 9 = 45000 and x 10 = 50000 = X .
