We have split the total allowed journey time of 3000 s into two timed sub-journeys of 1275 s and 1725 s, respectively. This split is approximately optimal for each train. It is known (Howlett, 2016) that the optimal split is when the optimal driving speeds are the same on each sub-journey. For the leading train the optimal times for the two journeys are T 2\u202f\u2248\u202f1276 and T 3\u202f\u2248\u202f1724 with optimal driving speeds V 2 = V 3 \u2248 22.3313 , optimal braking speeds U 2 = U 3 \u2248 10.2984 and cost J \u2113\u202f\u2248\u202f854.402. For the following train the optimal times for the two journeys are T 2\u202f\u2248\u202f1282 and T 3\u202f\u2248\u202f1718 with V 2 = V 3 \u2248 23.0385 and cost Jf \u202f\u2248\u202f864.984. On the first leg the following train uses a strategy of maximum acceleration to speed V 2 * \u2248 22.3291 < V 2 , coast and maximum brake. The train never reaches the optimal driving speed on this first leg. See Albrecht et al. (2016, Section 2.3) for details of strategies of this type. Braking begins at the optimal braking speeds U 2 = U 3 \u2248 10.8263 . The above details show that our split is approximately optimal for each train. \u25a1 11 Optimal switching for phases of regular control using the adjoint variable The notes in this section apply to both the leading train and the following train. The regular control modes are maximum acceleration, coast and maximum brake. Phases of maximum acceleration with u = u ( v ) = H ( v ) are used to speed up at the beginning of an optimal journey and to traverse steep uphill segments. Phases of coast with u = u ( v ) = 0 are used to traverse steep downhill segments and to slow down near the end of the journey. A phase of maximum brake with u = u ( v ) = \u2212 K ( v ) is always used to complete an optimal journey. The train also uses a phase of regular control to transfer from speedhold with partial acceleration at speed Vi to speedhold with partial acceleration at speed V i + 1 when the train passes a signal location xi where there is an active intermediate time constraint. For the leading train this transfer phase will be a phase of coast whereas for the following train it will be a phase of maximum acceleration. Whenever a phase of singular control is interrupted by a phase of regular control the optimal switching points must be calculated. The optimal switching points for phases of regular control for both the leading train and the following train are determined by the evolution of the adjoint variable \u03b7 = \u03bc / v \u2212 1 just as they are for the classic single train control problem with no intermediate time constraints (Albrecht et al., 2015a; 2015b). The difference here is that the differential equation for the adjoint variable may contain additional terms from active intermediate time constraints. 11.1 Phases of regular control with no active intermediate time constraints Consider an optimal phase of regular control on an interval (a, d) to traverse a steep segment of track ( b , c ) \u2286 ( a , d ) \u2286 ( x i , x i + 1 ) . This means there are no active intermediate time constraints in the interval (a, d). It follows from (11) and (21) for the leading train or from (11) and (28) for the following train that (35) d \u03b7 d x + v 2 u \u2032 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( v ) v 3 \u03b7 = \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i ) v 3 for all x\u202f\u2208\u202f[a, d]. Thus the adjoint equation has exactly the same form as it does in the classic single train problem and the calculation of optimal switching points is precisely the same. See Albrecht et al. (2015a, 2015b) for a comprehensive treatment of this problem. 11.2 Phases of regular control with one active intermediate time constraint Consider an optimal phase of regular control on an interval (a, d) with a \u2208 ( x i \u2212 1 , x i ) and d \u2208 ( x i , x i + 1 ) . Thus there is one active constraint in (a, d). A phase of this type could be required to traverse a steep segment of track (b, c)\u2286(a, d) or it could simply be required to transfer from a phase of speedhold at the optimal driving speed V i \u2212 1 in the region x < q i \u2212 1 = a to a phase of speedhold at the optimal driving speed Vi in the region x > p i = d . In either case it follows from (11) and (21) for the leading train or from (11) and (28) for the following train that (36) d \u03b7 d x + v 2 u \u2032 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( v ) v 3 \u03b7 = \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i \u2212 1 ) v 3 \u03c7 ( a , x i ) + \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i ) v 3 \u03c7 ( x i , d ) for all x\u202f\u2208\u202f[a, d]. Thus the adjoint equation has a more complicated form than it does in the classic single train problem. In all other respects the procedure for calculating optimal switching points is exactly the same (Albrecht et al., 2015a; 2015b). In particular we need to choose (a, d) so that \u03b7 ( a ) = \u03b7 ( d ) = 0 . 11.3 Phases of regular control with two active intermediate time constraints Consider an optimal phase of regular control on an interval (a, d) with a \u2208 ( x i \u2212 1 , x i ) and d \u2208 ( x i + 1 , x i + 2 ) . Thus there are two active constraints in (a, d). A phase of this type could be required to traverse a steep segment of track (b, c)\u2286(a, d). Let t ( x i ) = k i and t ( x i + 1 ) = k i + 1 > k i where ki and k i + 1 are the prescribed clearance times. Let w = w ( x , s ) denote the unique solution to the equation of motion defined by w ( x i , s ) = U + \u03f5 s where U is fixed, s is a real parameter and \u03f5\u202f>\u202f0 is a fixed scale factor. If v ( x ) = w ( x , s ) then the train reaches x i + 1 at time t s ( x i + 1 ) = k i + \u222b x i x i + 1 d x w ( x , s ) . If s 1\u202f>\u202fs 2 then w(x, s 1)\u202f>\u202fw(x, s 2) for all x\u202f\u2208\u202f(a, d). Therefore t s ( x i + 1 ) decreases as s increases. Hence there is at most one value of s with t s ( x i + 1 ) = k i + 1 . Suppose both time constraints are satisfied when s = s 0 . If we define v 0 ( x ) = w ( x , s 0 ) then k i + 1 = k i + \u222b x i x i + 1 d x v 0 ( x ) . The speed profile v 0 is uniquely defined but the initial speed constraint v 0 ( a ) = V i \u2212 1 and the final speed constraint v 0 ( d ) = V i + 1 will still define a range of different intervals (a, d) for different optimal driving speeds { V i \u2212 1 , V i + 1 } . The interval (a, d) is optimal only if \u03b7 ( a ) = \u03b7 ( d ) = 0 . It follows from (11) and (21) for the leading train or from (11) and (28) for the following train that (37) d \u03b7 d x + v 0 2 u \u2032 ( v 0 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( v 0 ) v 0 3 \u03b7 = \u03c8 ( v 0 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i \u2212 1 ) v 0 3 \u03c7 ( a , x i ) + \u03c8 ( v 0 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i ) v 0 3 \u03c7 ( x i , x i + 1 ) + \u03c8 ( v 0 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i + 1 ) v 0 3 \u03c7 ( x i + 1 , d ) for all x\u202f\u2208\u202f[a, d]. If we now imagine that V i \u2212 1 and V i + 1 are fixed then (a, d) is also fixed. If we set \u03b7 ( a ) = 0 then it may be possible to adjust the value of Vi so that \u03b7 ( d ) = 0 . Note that Vi must lie between V i \u2212 1 and V i + 1 . We will show later that a range of different optimal intervals is possible. 11.4 Phases of regular control with more than two active intermediate time constraints In essence such phases cannot occur. We have already seen in Section 11.3 that the speed profile for an optimal phase of regular control on an interval (a, d) is completely determined by two active intermediate time constraints. The two active constraints define a unique speed profile v = v 0 ( x ) and a corresponding unique time profile t 0 ( x ) = t 0 ( a ) + \u222b a x d \u03be v 0 ( \u03be ) for all x\u202f\u2208\u202f[a, d]. If another time constraint is imposed then it will almost surely lie strictly above or strictly below the existing time profile in which case it is either inactive or infeasible. In the unlikely event that it lies on the existing time profile it is simply superfluous. 12 Optimal switching for phases of regular control using the local energy functional A useful alternative necessary condition for optimal switching for phases of regular control on an interval (a, d) can be given in terms of a local energy functional. If we integrate the adjoint equation over (a, d) then the condition \u03b7 ( a ) = \u03b7 ( d ) = 0 can be replaced by an equivalent integral condition. It was shown in Albrecht et al. (2013a, 2015a, 2015b) and Howlett et al. (2009) that the integral is the derivative of a special local energy functional and that the local energy functional is minimized under optimal switching. A local energy functional can also be defined for this problem. 12.1 The local energy functional with no active intermediate time constraints Let v = v ( x ) denote the optimal speed profile for a phase of regular control on an interval (a, d) to traverse a steep segment ( b , c ) \u2286 ( a , d ) \u2286 ( x i , x i + 1 ) with v ( a ) = v ( d ) = V i . Consider a perturbed speed profile w = w ( x , s ) on an interval (a(s), d(s)) defined by a perturbation parameter s and the condition w ( a , s ) = V i + \u03f5 s where \u03f5\u202f>\u202f0 is a scale factor. The interval (a(s), d(s)) is chosen so that w [ a ( s ) , s ] = w [ d ( s ) , s ] = V i . We note that w ( x , 0 ) = v ( x ) with a ( 0 ) = a and d ( 0 ) = d . The local energy functional is defined by (38) J loc ( s ) = \u222b a ( s ) d ( s ) [ E i ( w ) \u2212 E i ( V i ) ] d x where the energy density function Ei (v) for the optimal driving speed Vi is defined by (39) E i ( v ) = \u03c8 ( V i ) v + r ( v ) for all v\u202f>\u202f0. Note that E i \u2032 ( v ) = [ \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i ) ] / v 2 for all v\u202f>\u202f0. If we differentiate (38) and set s = 0 then w ( x , 0 ) = v ( x ) is the optimal speed profile and [ \u2202 w / \u2202 s ] ( x , 0 ) = \u03b4 v ( x ) > 0 is the scaled infinitesimal perturbation of the optimal speed profile (Albrecht et al., 2013a; 2015a; 2015b; Howlett et al., 2009). In this case the alternative necessary condition for optimal switching is given by (40) J loc \u2032 ( 0 ) = \u03f5 \u222b a d E i \u2032 ( v ) \u03b4 v d x = 0 . This condition can also be obtained by integrating the adjoint equation over the interval (a, d). See Albrecht et al. (2013a, 2015a, 2015b), Howlett et al. (2009) and Howlett (2016) for an expanded discussion of the local energy functional. See also Birkhoff and Rota (1978) for the relevant mathematical background. In this case\u2014with no active intermediate time constraint\u2014these conditions are exactly the same as in the classic single train problem. 12.2 The local energy functional with one active intermediate time constraints Let v = v ( x ) denote the optimal speed profile for a phase of regular control on an interval (a, d) with a \u2208 ( x i \u2212 1 , x i ) and d \u2208 ( x i , x i + 1 ) . We have one active constraint in (a, d) and we require v ( a ) = V i \u2212 1 and v ( d ) = V i . Let w = w ( x , s ) be the perturbed speed profile defined on an interval (a(s), d(s)) by a perturbation parameter s and the condition w ( a , s ) = V i \u2212 1 + \u03f5 s where \u03f5\u202f>\u202f0 is a scale factor. The interval (a(s), d(s)) is chosen so that w [ a ( s ) , s ] = V i \u2212 1 and w [ d ( s ) , s ] = V i . The local energy functional is defined by (41) J loc ( s ) = \u222b a ( s ) x i [ E i \u2212 1 ( w ) \u2212 E i \u2212 1 ( V i \u2212 1 ) ] d x + \u222b x i d ( s ) [ E i ( w ) \u2212 E i ( V i ) ] d x and the alternative necessary condition for optimal switching is given by (42) J loc \u2032 ( 0 ) = \u03f5 [ \u222b a x i E i \u2212 1 \u2032 ( v ) \u03b4 v d x + \u222b x i d E i \u2032 ( v ) \u03b4 v d x ] = 0 . This condition can also be obtained by integrating the adjoint equation over the interval (a, d). A typical application of (42) occurs when there are no steep segments in the interval ( x i \u2212 1 , x i + 1 ) but the train must transfer from speedhold at the optimal driving speed V i \u2212 1 for x < a = q i \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x i \u2212 1 , x i ) to speedhold at the optimal driving speed Vi for x > d = p i \u2208 ( x i , x i + 1 ) . For the leading train we have V \u2113 , i \u2212 1 > V \u2113 , i and the train must use a phase of coast on an interval ( q \u2113 , i \u2212 1 , p \u2113 , i ) \u2286 ( x i \u2212 1 , x i + 1 ) with x i \u2208 ( q \u2113 , i \u2212 1 , p \u2113 , i ) . Since the track is not steep downhill the speed must decrease throughout the interval ( q \u2113 , i \u2212 1 , p \u2113 , i ) with V \u2113 , i \u2212 1 = v \u2113 ( q \u2113 , i \u2212 1 ) > v \u2113 ( x ) > v \u2113 ( p \u2113 , i ) = V \u2113 , i for all x \u2208 ( q \u2113 , i \u2212 1 , p \u2113 , i ) . The condition (42) can be rewritten as (43) \u222b q \u2113 , i \u2212 1 x i \u03c8 ( v \u2113 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V \u2113 , i \u2212 1 ) v \u2113 2 \u03b4 v \u2113 d x + \u222b x i p \u2113 , i \u03c8 ( v \u2113 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V \u2113 , i ) v \u2113 2 \u03b4 v \u2113 d x = 0 . To solve (43) we must adjust the position of the interval ( q \u2113 , i \u2212 1 , p \u2113 , i ) . The condition V \u2113 , i \u2212 1 \u2265 v \u2113 ( x ) \u2265 V \u2113 , i for all x \u2208 ( q \u2113 , i \u2212 1 , p \u2113 , i ) shows that the first integral on the left-hand side is negative while the second integral is positive. If we choose the coast interval as early as possible so that p \u2113 , i = x i then the second integral vanishes and the left-hand side of (43) is negative. If we choose the coast interval as late as possible so that q \u2113 , i \u2212 1 = x i then the first integral vanishes and the left-hand side of (43) is positive. Hence there must be a unique solution to (43) between these two extremes with x i \u2208 ( q \u2113 , i \u2212 1 , p \u2113 , i ) . A similar argument applies to the following train except that V f , i \u2212 1 < V f , i and so the following train must use a phase of maxumum acceleration on an interval ( q f , i \u2212 1 , p f , i ) with x i \u2208 ( q f , i \u2212 1 , p f , i ) to transfer from speedhold at the optimal driving speed V f , i \u2212 1 for x < q f , i \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x i \u2212 1 , x i ) to speedhold at the optimal driving speed V f,i for x > p f , i \u2208 ( x i , x i + 1 ) . 12.3 The local energy functional with two active intermediate time constraints Let v = v ( x ) denote the optimal speed profile for a phase of regular control on an interval (a, d) with a \u2208 ( x i \u2212 1 , x i ) and d \u2208 ( x i + 1 , x i + 2 ) . We have two active constraints in (a, d) and we require v ( a ) = V i \u2212 1 and v ( d ) = V i + 1 . The speed profile is fixed by the two time constraints and so the position of the interval (a, d) will be determined by the values of V i \u2212 1 , Vi and V i + 1 . As before we let w = w ( x , s ) be a perturbed speed profile defined on an interval (a(s), d(s)) by a parameter s and the condition w ( a , s ) = V i \u2212 1 + \u03f5 s where \u03f5\u202f>\u202f0 is a scale factor. This time we choose (a(s), d(s)) so that w [ a ( s ) , s ] = V i \u2212 1 and w [ d ( s ) , s ] = V i + 1 . The local energy functional is defined by (44) J loc ( s ) = \u222b a ( s ) x i [ E i \u2212 1 ( w ) \u2212 E i \u2212 1 ( V i \u2212 1 ) ] d x + \u222b x i x i + 1 [ E i ( w ) \u2212 E i ( V i ) ] d x + \u222b x i + 1 d ( s ) [ E i + 1 ( w ) \u2212 E i + 1 ( V i + 1 ) ] d x and the alternative necessary condition for optimal switching is given by (45) J loc \u2032 ( 0 ) = \u03f5 [ \u222b a x i E i \u2212 1 \u2032 ( v ) \u03b4 v d x + \u222b x i x i + 1 E i \u2032 ( v ) \u03b4 v d x + \u222b x i + 1 d E i + 1 \u2032 ( v ) \u03b4 v d x ] = 0 . This condition can also be obtained by integrating the adjoint equation over the interval (a, d). The values of V i \u2212 1 , Vi and V i + 1 determine the precise position for the optimal phase of regular control. In order to discuss some of the key deas we will show that it is possible to have a = b and c = d . We will also show that it is possible to have a = b and c\u202f<\u202fd. The case a\u202f<\u202fb and c = d is also possible but the derivation will be left to the reader. 12.3.1 The regular control phase coincides exactly with the steep segment For the sake of argument we consider the leading train on a steep uphill segment but nevertheless use a general notation. We will show that if ( x i , x i + 1 ) \u2286 ( b , c ) then it is possible to have an optimal strategy with a speedhold segment at the optimal driving speed V i \u2212 1 for x\u202f<\u202fb, a phase of maximum acceleration for x\u202f\u2208\u202f(b, c) and a phase of speedhold at the optimal driving speed V i + 1 for x\u202f>\u202fc. The timing constraints t ( x i ) = k i and t ( x i + 1 ) = k i + 1 mean that (46) k i + 1 = k i + \u222b x i x i + 1 d x v where v = v ( x ) denotes the optimal speed profile with v ( x ) = V i \u2212 1 for x\u202f\u2264\u202fb and v ( x ) = V i + 1 for x\u202f\u2265\u202fc. We showed in Section 11.3 that there is only one speed profile v = v ( x ) for a phase of maximum acceleration which satisfies (46) and so this single constraint also determines the speeds v b = v ( b ) and v c = v ( c ) at the beginning and end of the steep segment. The necessary condition (45) for optimality can be rewritten in the form (47) J loc \u2032 ( 0 ) = \u03f5 [ \u222b b x i \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i \u2212 1 ) v 2 \u03b4 v d x + \u222b x i x i + 1 \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i ) v 2 \u03b4 v d x + \u222b x i + 1 c \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i + 1 ) v 2 \u03b4 v d x ] = 0 . Because (b, c) is steep uphill we know that V i \u2212 1 > v ( x ) > V i + 1 for all x\u202f\u2208\u202f(b, c). Therefore the first integral in (47) is negative provided b\u202f<\u202fxi and the third integral is positive provided x i + 1 < c . The remaining integral in (47) can be either positive or negative depending on the value of Vi . If it happens that (48) J loc \u2032 ( 0 ) | V i = V i \u2212 1 = \u03f5 [ \u222b b x i + 1 \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i \u2212 1 ) v 2 \u03b4 v d x + \u222b x i + 1 c \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i + 1 ) v 2 \u03b4 v d x ] < 0 and that (49) J loc \u2032 ( 0 ) | V i = V i + 1 = \u03f5 [ \u222b b x i \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i \u2212 1 ) v 2 \u03b4 v d x + \u222b x i c \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i + 1 ) v 2 \u03b4 v d x ] > 0 then since J loc \u2032 ( 0 ) decreases as Vi increases we can find a uniquely-determined value V i \u2208 ( V i + 1 , V i \u2212 1 ) such that J loc \u2032 ( 0 ) = 0 . Thus (47) has a unique solution for some V i \u2208 ( V i + 1 , V i \u2212 1 ) . Note that the conditions (48) and (49) are certainly true if b = x i and c = x i + 1 . Thus the optimal strategy consists of speedhold at the optimal driving speed V i \u2212 1 for x < b = x i , maximum acceleration for x \u2208 ( b , c ) = ( x i , x i + 1 ) and speedhold at the optimal driving speed speed V i + 1 for x > x i + 1 = c . A similar argument applies to an optimal coast phase for the following train on a steep downhill segment. If b = x i and c = x i + 1 and we also know that g ( x ) = \u03b3 i is constant for all x \u2208 ( b , c ) = ( x i , x i + 1 ) then the adjoint variable is given by an algebraic formula \u03b7 i ( v ) = \u03c8 ( V i ) / v + r ( v ) \u2212 \u03b4 i H ( v ) \u2212 r ( v ) + \u03b3 i for x \u2208 ( b , c ) = ( x i , x i + 1 ) where v = v ( x ) is the corresponding speed profile and \u03b4i is a constant. See Albrecht et al. (2015a, 2015b) and Howlett et al. (2009) for more details. If the track is not steep for x \u2209 ( b , c ) = ( x i , x i + 1 ) then v ( x i ) = V i \u2212 1 and v ( x i + 1 ) = V i + 1 and the optimality conditions \u03b7 i ( V i ) = 0 and \u03b7 i ( V i + 1 ) = 0 can be used to eliminate \u03b4i and show that (50) \u03c8 ( V i ) = V i + 1 \u03c6 ( V i \u2212 1 ) \u2212 V i \u2212 1 \u03c6 ( V i + 1 ) V i \u2212 1 \u2212 V i + 1 . The convexity of \u03c6 ensures that (50) has a unique solution for V i \u2208 ( V i + 1 , V i \u2212 1 ) . 12.3.2 The regular control phase begins at the start of the steep segment but extends beyond it Once again we consider the leading train on a steep uphill segment and use a general notation. Let us initially suppose that there exists an optimal strategy for the leading train with a phase of maximum acceleration restricted to the steep uphill segment ( b , c ) \u2287 ( x i , x i + 1 ) as discussed in Section 12.3.1. Suppose further that the optimal driving speeds are V i \u2212 1 , 0 > V i , 0 > V i + 1 , 0 for the intervals ( x i \u2212 1 , x i ) , ( x i , x i + 1 ) and ( x i + 1 , x i + 2 ) , respectively, and that the optimal speed profile is denoted by v 0 = v 0 ( x ) with v 0 ( x ) = V i \u2212 1 , 0 for x\u202f<\u202fb and v 0 ( x ) = V i + 1 , 0 for x\u202f>\u202fc. Define V i \u2212 1 ( \u03b1 ) = V i \u2212 1 , 0 and V i ( \u03b1 ) = V i + 1 ( \u03b1 ) = ( 1 \u2212 \u03b1 ) V i + 1 , 0 + \u03b1 V i , 0 for each \u03b1\u202f\u2208\u202f(0, 1). A stylized speed profile v 0 = v 0 ( x ) with maximum acceleration restricted to the steep segment (b, c) and a corresponding stylized optimal speed profile w \u03b1 = w \u03b1 ( x ) for some \u03b1\u202f\u2208\u202f(0, 1) are shown in Fig. 2 . Now if we substitute \u03b1 = 0 we have V i ( 0 ) = V i + 1 ( 0 ) = V i + 1 , 0 and d ( 0 ) = c . Therefore J loc , 0 \u2032 ( 0 ) \u2212 J loc \u2032 ( 0 ) = [ \u222b x i x i + 1 \u03c8 ( V i , 0 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i + 1 , 0 ) w 0 2 \u03b4 w 0 d x ] > 0 . If we substitute \u03b1 = 1 then we have V i ( 1 ) = V i + 1 ( 1 ) = V i , 0 and hence J loc , 1 \u2032 ( 0 ) \u2212 J loc \u2032 ( 0 ) = \u03f5 [ \u222b x i + 1 c \u03c8 ( V i + 1 , 0 ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i , 0 ) w 1 2 \u03b4 w 1 d x + \u222b c d ( 1 ) \u03c8 ( v ) \u2212 \u03c8 ( V i , 0 ) w 1 2 \u03b4 w 1 d x ] < 0 . Since J loc , \u03b1 \u2032 ( 0 ) is continuous and strictly decreasing in \u03b1 it follows that there is a unique \u03b1\u202f\u2208\u202f(0, 1) such that (53) is satisfied. Thus there is a phase of speedhold with partial acceleration at speed V i \u2212 1 for x\u202f<\u202fb\u202f\u2264\u202fxi , a phase of maximum acceleration on the interval (b, d(\u03b1)) and a phase of speedhold with partial acceleration at speed V i + 1 ( \u03b1 ) for x > d ( \u03b1 ) > x i + 1 . Similar considerations apply to a coast phase for the following train on a steep downhill segment. 12.4 Other considerations If regenerative braking is allowed and there are segments of track that are steep downhill at the optimal regenerative braking speed W \u2113,i for the leading train or W f,i for the following train then phases of speedhold with partial braking at the optimal regenerative braking speed must be interrupted by coast phases to traverse any intermediate subsections that are not steep downhill at the optimal regenerative braking speed. The optimal switching points for such interruptions are found in the usual way. For an extended discussion of the general principles for optimal switching we refer to Albrecht et al. (2013a, 2015a, 2015b) and Howlett et al. (2009). If the steep segments within any particular interval ( x i , x i + 1 ) are too extensive then it may not be possible to hold the optimal driving speed Vi at any point in the interval. To a certain extent these situations require a more specific set of assumptions about the precise form of the gradient profile. This is the case for the classic single train problem too. Nevertheless these more complex situations can always be treated in the usual way as an interruption to the basic optimal but infeasible strategy that would have been used for non-steep track. Since optimal driving speeds are not known a priori such situations can only be truly resolved in specific circumstances. 13 The state constraints In our theoretical application of the Pontryagin principle it was convenient to assume that all constraints were active. Thus we omitted inactive constraints from the analysis. In practice it is usually more convenient in terms of the notation to retain the inactive constraints. If so then the sequences of optimal driving speeds are simply decreasing and increasing respectively\u2014rather than strictly decreasing and strictly increasing. If the subsequence of signal locations corresponding to active constraints for the leading train is denoted by { x \u2113 , i ( r ) } r = 1 p and the subsequence for the following train is denoted by { x f , j ( s ) } s = 0 q then we deduced that the subsequence { V \u2113 , i ( r ) } r = 0 p \u2212 1 of optimal driving speeds for the leading train on ( x \u2113 , i ( r ) , x \u2113 , i ( r + 1 ) ) was strictly decreasing and that the subsequence { V f , j ( s ) } s = 0 q \u2212 1 of optimal driving speeds for the following train on ( x f , j ( s ) , x f , j ( s + 1 ) ) was strictly increasing. More specifically we have the following characterizations. If V \u2113 , i ( r ) \u2212 1 > V \u2113 , i ( r ) and V \u2113 , i ( r + 1 ) \u2212 1 > V \u2113 , i ( r + 1 ) and if t \u2113(xj )\u202f<\u202fh \u2113,j for each j = i ( r ) + 1 , \u2026 , i ( r + 1 ) \u2212 1 then ( x \u2113 , i ( r ) , x \u2113 , i ( r + 1 ) ) is an actively-timed segment for the leading train. We must have active constraints t \u2113 ( x \u2113 , i ( r ) ) = h \u2113 , i ( r ) and t \u2113 ( x i ( r + 1 ) ) = h \u2113 , i ( r + 1 ) at each endpoint and within the segment the optimal driving speeds must be equal with V \u2113 , i ( r ) = \u22ef = V \u2113 , i ( r + 1 ) \u2212 1 . If V f , j ( s ) \u2212 1 < V f , j ( s ) and V f , j ( s + 1 ) \u2212 1 < V f , j ( s + 1 ) and if tf (xj )\u202f>\u202fh f,j for each j = j ( s ) + 1 , \u2026 , j ( s + 1 ) \u2212 1 then ( x f , j ( s ) , x f , j ( s + 1 ) ) is an actively-timed segment for the following train. We must have active constraints t f ( x f , j ( s ) ) = h f , j ( s ) and t f ( x f , j ( s + 1 ) ) = h f , j ( s + 1 ) at each endpoint and within the segment the optimal driving speeds must be equal with V f , j ( s ) + 1 = \u22ef = V f , j ( s + 1 ) \u2212 1 . It follows that each decreasing sequence { V \u2113 , i } i = 0 n \u2212 1 of optimal driving speeds for the leading train defines a unique optimal control u \u2113 = u \u2113 ( x ) and corresponding unique optimal speed and time profiles ( v \u2113 , t \u2113 ) = ( v \u2113 ( x ) , t \u2113 ( x ) ) for each x\u202f\u2208\u202f[0, X]. Likewise each increasing sequence { V f , j } j = 1 n \u2212 1 of optimal driving speeds for the following train defines a unique optimal control u f = u f ( x ) with corresponding unique optimal speed and time profiles ( v f , t f ) = ( v f ( x ) , t f ( x ) ) for all x\u202f\u2208\u202f[0, X]. By considering different sequences of optimal driving speeds it is possible to adjust the speed and time profiles in order to satisfy any feasible set of prescribed section clearance times. 13.1 Determination of active constraints This is an important first step. Consider the leading train problem. For a given set of prescribed intermediate times the optimal strategy is determined by a decreasing sequence of optimal driving speeds { V \u2113 , j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 on each section. If V \u2113 , j \u2212 1 > V \u2113 , j then the time constraint t \u2113(xj )\u202f\u2264\u202fh \u2113,j is active and we must have t \u2113 ( x j ) = h \u2113 , j . If t \u2113(xj )\u202f<\u202fh \u2113,j then the time constraint is inactive and we must have V \u2113 , j \u2212 1 = V \u2113 , j . Any strategy in this form is a strategy of optimal type. In principle any such strategy that satisfies all of the time constraints is a potential solution. How should we determine which constraints are active when we search for the optimal solution? This is a common problem in the theory of constrained optimization where it is often necessary to investigate all possible alternatives. For train control problems there is a relatively straightforward answer to this dilemma. The most efficient driving phase is always a speedhold phase provided the phase is feasible. Thus the lowest cost strategy of optimal type is always the unrestricted optimal strategy where all intermediate constraints are inactive. In this case there is a single optimal driving speed V \u2113 ( 1 ) = V \u2113 , 0 = V \u2113 , 1 = \u22ef = V \u2113 , n \u2212 1 . Thus our first option should always be to find the unrestricted optimal strategy that satisfies the overall time constraint. This process requires selection of an optimal driving speed followed by calculation of the corresponding strategy of optimal type. The overall time constraint is then checked and if that constraint is not satisfied to sufficient accuracy the selected optimal driving speed is revised and the process repeated. When the overall time constraint is satisfied then the intermediate time constraints are checked. If none are violated then the unrestricted optimal strategy is the desired solution. If any one of the intermediate time constraints is violated then the unrestricted optimal strategy is not feasible and we must choose a more complex strategy of optimal type. Now select the intermediate time constraint that is most blatantly violated by the unrestricted optimal strategy. For convenience we will assume that this occurs at x = x q . We must now find two optimal driving speeds V \u2113 ( 2 ) = V \u2113 , 0 = \u22ef = V \u2113 , q \u2212 1 > W \u2113 ( 2 ) = V \u2113 , q = \u22ef = V \u2113 , n \u2212 1 where V \u2113 ( 2 ) > V \u2113 ( 1 ) > W \u2113 ( 2 ) such that the corresponding strategy of optimal type satisfies the overall time constraint and the equality t \u2113 ( x q ) = h \u2113 , q thereby satisfying the intermediate time constraint t \u2113(xq )\u202f\u2264\u202fh \u2113.q . Once again a searching process is required to find suitable optimal driving speeds. If no other intermediate time constraint is violated then the proposed strategy of optimal type is the optimal strategy. If any one of the remaining intermediate time constraints is violated then one must again seek a more complex strategy. Select the most blatantly violated of the remaining time constraints. For convenience in the description we will suppose that this constraint is at x = x p where xp \u202f<\u202fxq . Now search for three optimal driving speeds V \u2113 ( 3 ) = V \u2113 , 0 = \u22ef = V \u2113 , p \u2212 1 > W \u2113 ( 3 ) = V \u2113 , p = \u22ef = V \u2113 , q \u2212 1 > X \u2113 ( 3 ) = V \u2113 , q = \u22ef = V \u2113 , n \u2212 1 where V \u2113 ( 3 ) > V \u2113 ( 2 ) > W \u2113 ( 3 ) > X \u2113 ( 3 ) such that the overall time constraint is satisfied and such that t \u2113 ( x p ) = h \u2113 , p and t \u2113 ( x q ) = h \u2113 , q thereby satisfying the intermediate constraints t \u2113(xp )\u202f\u2264\u202fh \u2113,p and t \u2113(xq )\u202f\u2264\u202fh \u2113,q . If no other intermediate time constraint is violated then this is the optimal strategy. Otherwise continue the process until all intermediate time constraints are satisfied. A similar process is recommended for the following train. For the leading train the process of fixing the most blatantly violated constraint will often fix most or all of the succeeding violated time constraints. For the following train fixing the most blatantly violated constraint will often fix most or all of the preceding violated time constraints. For each train it is possible that all n \u2212 1 intermediate time constraints will be active and that the above procedure will need to be applied to every intermediate constraint. One might also imagine that inclusion of subsequent active constraints could conceivably cause a previously active constraint to become inactive and that this could happen repeatedly to the same constraint. However we know that the process to determine active constraints must be finite because if there are n \u2212 1 imposed constraints then there are 2 n \u2212 1 different combinations of active constraints. If necessary these combinations could all be tested individually. However this is far less efficient than the progressive procedure suggested above. In practice it is usually not necessary to compute a full strategy of optimal type at each stage. Preliminary approximations will often identify any additional active constraints. On track with no steep gradients the optimal strategy is essentially a strategy of speedhold at the optimal driving speed on each actively-timed segment. Hence the active constraints within any nominated segment can usually be determined by assuming a constant speed strategy that satisfies the overall time constraint on that segment and then determining which intermediate time constraints are not satisfied. This heuristic procedure will also often work on tracks with steep segments because the optimal driving speed is essentially a proxy for the holding speed that would be required if it were possible to hold a constant speed throughout the relevant segment. As well as searching for the active constraints it is also necessary to search for the optimal driving speeds. For numerical calculations we define \u03c4 j = t \u2113 ( x j ) \u2212 h \u2113 , j for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n and set a desired tolerance \u03c4\u202f>\u202f0 for all the intermediate time constraints. We will say that the intermediate time constraints for the leading train are satisfied to sufficient accuracy if \u03c4 j + \u03c4 < 0 for all j = 1 , \u2026 , n . Note that this definition also covers inactive constraints where \u03c4j \u202f<\u202f0. With this interpretation the search process for the optimal driving speeds will terminate after a finite number of steps. For the following train the optimal strategy is completely determined by a sequence of optimal driving speeds { V f , j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 with V f , j \u2264 V f , j + 1 where V f,j is the optimal driving speed on the interval ( x j , x j + 1 ) for each j = 0 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . If V f , j < V f , j + 1 then the time constraint tf (xj )\u202f\u2265\u202fh f,j is active and we must have t f ( x j ) = h f , j . If tf (xj )\u202f>\u202fh f,j then the time constraint is inactive and we must have V f , j = V f , j + 1 . Similar remarks to those for the leading train apply to the determination of active constraints and optimal driving speeds for the following train. 13.2 Speed limits and strategies of optimal type We have not considered speed limits in our model but there is no significant difficulty if speed limits are incorporated. With or without speed limits the basic problem remains the same. We must find a strategy of optimal type that satisfies the imposed time constraints. In the two-train separation problem each strategy of optimal type is uniquely determined by a sequence V = { V j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 of optimal driving speeds. If a speed limit v(x)\u202f\u2264\u202fvL (x) is imposed and if the speed v V ( x ) for an existing optimal strategy V exceeds the speed limit on any non-trivial interval then a new optimal strategy must be found. The new optimal strategy will be determined by a new sequence W = { W j } j = 0 n \u2212 1 of optimal driving speeds but will take the same general form at all locations where the speed limit is not active (Albrecht et al., 2015a; Khmelnitsky, 2000; Liu and Golovitcher, 2003). At locations where the speed limit is active then the new optimal profile must follow the speed limit with v W ( x ) = v L ( x ) . At locations where the speed limit is not active the speed will be higher than it was for the unlimited problem with v V ( x ) < v W ( x ) < v L ( x ) . The rules for optimal switching remain the same when the speed limit is not active but a jump discontinuity may occur in the value of the adjoint variable when entering or leaving a speed limit regime (Hartl et al., 1995). 14 Track with piecewise constant gradient acceleration Most railways use a piecewise-constant approximation to the continuous gradient acceleration. It is known (Howlett et al., 2009; Liu and Golovitcher, 2003; Vu, 2006) that on track with piecewise constant gradient the adjoint variable can be expressed as an algebraic function of the speed. In this case the integrals in the optimal switching conditions can be evaluated analytically provided care is taken to ensure that the continuity of the adjoint variable is preserved. We illustrate the process by considering an intermediate phase of regular control with one active intermediate time constraint. Consider an intermediate phase of regular control on an interval ( q i \u2212 1 , p i ) where q \u2212 1 \u2208 ( x i \u2212 1 , x i ) and p i \u2208 ( x i , x i + 1 ) with an active timing constraint t ( x i ) = k i . Suppose we have points { \u03be r } r = 0 m + 1 with \u03be 0 < q i \u2212 1 < \u03be 1 < \u22ef < \u03be m < p i < \u03be m + 1 such that g ( x ) = \u03b3 r when x \u2208 ( \u03be r , \u03be r + 1 ) for each r = 0 , \u2026 , m . We will write v r = v ( \u03be r ) for all r = 1 , \u2026 , m \u2212 1 . For x \u2208 ( q i \u2212 1 , p i ) we have control u = u ( v ) with either u = H ( v ) for a phase of maximum acceleration or u = 0 for a phase of coast. The optimal speed profile is denoted by v = v ( x ) with v ( q i \u2212 1 ) = V i \u2212 1 and v ( p i ) = V i . Let w = w ( x , s ) be the perturbed speed profile defined by w ( q i \u2212 1 , s ) = V i \u2212 1 + \u03f5 s where \u03f5\u202f>\u202f0 is a scale factor. It follows from (11) that \u2202 \u2202 s [ w \u2202 w \u2202 x ] = ( u \u2032 ( w ) \u2212 r \u2032 ( w ) ) \u2202 w \u2202 s \u21d4 \u2202 \u2202 x [ w \u2202 w \u2202 s ] = u \u2032 ( w ) \u2212 r \u2032 ( w ) w \u00b7 w \u2202 w \u2202 s and so for x \u2208 ( \u03be r , \u03be r + 1 ) we have w \u2202 w \u2202 s = exp [ \u222b u \u2032 ( w ) \u2212 r \u2032 ( w ) w d x ] = exp [ \u222b u \u2032 ( w ) \u2212 r \u2032 ( w ) w \u00b7 w d w u ( w ) \u2212 r ( w ) + \u03b3 r ] = c r ( u ( w ) \u2212 r ( w ) + \u03b3 r ) from which we see that \u2202 w / \u2202 s = c r \u2202 w / \u2202 x where cr is a constant of integration. If we set s = 0 this means that the infinitesimal perturbation \u03b4 v = \u03b4 v ( x ) is given by \u03b4 v = c r d v / d x for all x \u2208 ( \u03be r , \u03be r + 1 ) . When we use this formula we must remember that continuity of \u03b4v(x) at x = \u03be r means (54) c r \u2212 1 [ u ( v r ) \u2212 r ( v r ) + \u03b3 r \u2212 1 ] = c r [ u ( v r ) \u2212 r ( v r ) + \u03b3 r ] for each r = 1 , \u2026 , m \u2212 1 . The initial magnitude of the infinitesimal perturbation is not especially important so the constants {cr } can be determined recursively by selecting a convenient value for c 0 and applying (54). For any interval ( p , q ) \u2286 ( \u03be r , \u03be r + 1 ) and any differentiable function E(v) it follows that (55) \u222b p q E \u2032 ( v ) \u03b4 v d x = c r \u222b p q E \u2032 ( v ) d v d x d x = c r [ E ( v q ) \u2212 E ( v p ) ] Suppose that x i \u2208 ( \u03be s , \u03be s + 1 ) . If we apply (55) to each subinterval of constant gradient then the optimal switching condition (42) can be rewritten as (56) c 0 [ E i \u2212 1 ( v 1 ) \u2212 E i \u2212 1 ( V i \u2212 1 ) ] + \u2211 r = 1 s \u2212 1 c r [ E i \u2212 1 ( v r + 1 ) \u2212 E i \u2212 1 ( v r ) ] + c s [ E i \u2212 1 ( U i ) \u2212 E i \u2212 1 ( v s ) ] + c s [ E i ( v s + 1 ) \u2212 E i ( U i ) ] + \u2211 r = s + 1 m \u2212 1 c r [ E i ( v r + 1 ) \u2212 E i ( v r ) ] + c m [ E i ( V i ) \u2212 E i ( v m ) ] = 0 where V i \u2212 1 = v ( q i \u2212 1 ) , V i = v ( p i ) and U i = v ( x i ) . The condition (56) is a necessary condition for an optimal intermediate phase of regular control with one active intermediate time constraint on track with piecewise constant gradient acceleration. 15 Optimal strategies for an elementary but realistic example There are currently no effective numerical algorithms specifically designed to solve the two-train separation problem. Existing algorithms can be used to find optimal strategies on each timed section provided the initial and final section speeds are known. To adapt these algorithms to the two-train separation problem, one must postulate a sequence of provisional optimal driving speeds using strategies of optimal type with plausible initial and final speeds for each timed section and then make ad hoc adjustments to these speeds until a feasible strategy that satisfies the intermediate section clearance times is found. The main difficulty is that the initial and final speeds and the optimal driving speed for any individual timed section all depend on the values of the corresponding parameters for each adjacent timed section. It is useful to consider an alternative viewpoint. We refer specifically to the leading train but similar remarks apply to the following train. Every strictly decreasing sequence of optimal driving speeds will generate a sequence of optimal sectional strategies provided that the final values of the speed and the modified adjoint variables on the previous section are equal to the corresponding initial values on the current section\u2014and provided that the required transition phases of regular control satisfy the conditions for optimal switching. In this way one can find a strategy of optimal type for the leading train that satisfies the general requirements for optimality. If one calculates the section clearance times for this strategy then the general uniqueness theorem (Albrecht et al., 2013a; 2015b; Khmelnitsky, 2000) shows that this strategy is the unique optimal strategy for those calculated clearance times and the associated sequence of initial and final section speeds. In this regard it is important to recall that the speeds at the ends of the timed sections U 1 = v ( x 1 ) , \u2026 , U n \u2212 1 = v ( x n \u2212 1 ) are uniquely determined by the sequence of optimal driving speeds and the optimal switching conditions. Ultimately this means one can find optimal strategies by first finding a strategy of optimal type and then progressively adjusting the optimal driving speeds for each section until one obtains sufficiently accurate approximations to the prescribed intermediate clearance times. When making the relevant calculations the inactive constraints can be omitted. The following example uses the same train parameters and the same track details as those used in Examples 1 and 2. We will choose specified clearance times that lie within the bounds generated by the extreme strategies in Example 2. The speeds at the intermediate signal locations are denoted by U = ( U 1 , \u2026 , U 13 ) and are given by U 1 = \u22ef = U 7 = 18.1994 , U 8\u202f\u2248\u202f16.9435, U 9\u202f\u2248\u202f18.3291, U 10\u202f\u2248\u202f19.8028, U 11 = 18.1994 , U 12\u202f\u2248\u202f22.5950 and U 13\u202f\u2248\u202f19.6366. The optimal braking speed is U 14\u202f\u2248\u202f14.9683. The calculated elapsed times at the signal locations are denoted by \u03b4 t = ( \u03b4 t 0 , \u2026 , \u03b4 t 14 ) where \u03b4 t = ( 0 , 446 , 886 , 1051 , 1224 , 1389 , 1558 , 1780 , 2006 , 2299 , 2674 , 2908 , 3120 , 3418 , 3840 ) . The actual times t = \u03b4 t + 720 \u00d7 1 are therefore t = ( 720 , 1166 , 1606 , 1771 , 1944 , 2109 , 2278 , 2500 , 2726 , 3019 , 3394 , 3628 , 3840 , 4138 , 4560 ) . Hence the time constraints t j \u2212 1 \u2265 h j are satisfied for all j = 1 , \u2026 , 13 . Therefore our proposed optimal journey is feasible. We use (6) and (7) to show that the cost of the strategy is Jf \u202f\u2248\u202f1074.16. The total cost for both trains is J = J \u2113 + J f \u2248 2153.91 . A stylized train graph is shown in Fig. 5. We emphasize that the separation conditions are violated if both trains use the unrestricted optimal strategy( Figs. 3\u20135 ). \u25a1 15.1 Particular comments about the solution to Example 4 The total journey time was chosen so that excessive speeds were not required. Faster journeys are possible but of course the cost increases. The clearance times were chosen in an ad hoc but seemingly reasonable manner based on approximate estimates for the time taken for the leading train to clear the signal locations while driving at a reasonable average speed. The trains are identical but although the detailed strategies depend on the train parameters the general form of the strategies does not. Similar calculations would apply with the same intermediate clearance times if the train parameters were changed. The leading train must complete the first two sections in at most 6 min. The following train must not leave before this time. The leading train must complete the entire journey in 64 min and so the earliest entry time to the final two sections for the following train is 64 min. The following train can do this using a strategy of maximum acceleration followed by speedhold from ( x , v ) = ( 2469 , 18.1994 ) to ( x , v ) = ( 33812 , 18.1994 ) , coast down the steep hill and then speedhold from ( x , v ) = ( 49636 , 18.1994 ) to ( x , v ) = ( 57344 , 18.1994 ) . It turns out that none of the intermediate time constraints at x = x j for j = 1 , \u2026 , 11 are active. The first active constraint is at x 12. The following train must now complete the final two sections in at most 6 min. This means that the only feasible strategy of optimal type on the final timed segment is one with phases of maximum acceleration, coast and brake. 16 Extension to the three-train separation problem The aim of this section is to show that the methods used for the two-train separation problem can be extended to more general train separation problems. 16.1 The intermediate time constraints For the three-train separation problem we assume that the specified intermediate times are given as two lists\u2014one list { h \u2113 , m , j } j = 0 n to separate the leading train from the middle train and another list { h m , f , j } j = 0 n to separate the middle train from the following train. The leading train must pass the location x j + 2 before time h \u2113 , m , j + 1 and the middle train must pass the location xj after time h \u2113 , m , j + 1 . The middle train must pass the location xj before time h m , f , j \u2212 1 and the following train must pass the location x j \u2212 2 after time h m , f , j \u2212 1 . For the leading train we have the time constraints (57) t \u2113 ( x j + 2 ) = \u222b 0 x j + 2 d x v \u2113 ( x ) \u2264 h \u2113 , m , j + 1 for each j = 0 , \u2026 , n \u2212 2 . For the middle train the time constraints are (58) h min , j = h \u2113 , m , j + 1 \u2264 t m ( x j ) = t m ( 0 ) + \u222b 0 x j d x v m ( x ) \u2264 h m , f , j \u2212 1 = h max , j for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 where we have defined h min , j = h \u2113 , m , j + 1 and h max , j = h m , f , j \u2212 1 . For the following train we have the time constraints (59) h m , f , j \u2212 1 \u2264 t f ( 0 ) + \u222b 0 x j \u2212 2 d x v f ( x ) = t f ( x j \u2212 2 ) for each j = 2 , \u2026 , n . We will not delve into the full details of what is required for the specified times but it is already clear that we must have h min , j = h \u2113 , m , j + 1 \u2264 h m , f , j \u2212 1 = h max , j for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . The intermediate time constraints for both the leading train and the following train have the same general form as they did in the two-train separation problem and hence the optimal strategies also have the same general form. That is the optimal strategy for the leading train is uniquely defined by a decreasing sequence of optimal driving speeds while the optimal strategy for the following train is uniquely defined by an increasing sequence of optimal driving speeds. The optimal strategy for the middle train is less straightforward because it is constrained by the strategy for the leading train and by the strategy for the following train. 16.2 The Pontryagin analysis For the middle train we assume that h min , 0 = \u0394 T 1 is the earliest allowed starting time and that h max , n + 1 = T + \u0394 T 1 is the latest allowed finishing time. From a mathematical point of view it is convenient to assume that h min,j \u202f<\u202fh max,j for all j = 0 , \u2026 , n + 1 . Thus, at each location xj , it is impossible for both constraints in (58) to be binding. This is an important point in our subsequent Pontryagin analysis. If neither constraint is binding at x = x j then the constraints and the location xj will be omitted from the list of active constraints in the mathematical analysis. The decision about whether or not the constraints at certain locations can be omitted is essentially a computational issue. Our assumption that h min,j \u202f<\u202fh max,j simply means that the case h min , j = h max , j must be solved approximately by setting h min , j + \u03c4 j = h max , j for some suitably small \u03c4j \u202f>\u202f0. Let H min = { j \u2208 [ 0 , n + 1 ] \u2223 t ( x j ) = \u222b 0 x j d x v ( x ) = h min , j } and H max = { j \u2208 [ 0 , n + 1 ] \u2223 t ( x j ) = \u222b 0 x j d x v ( x ) = h max , j } where we have written t ( x ) = t m ( x ) and v ( x ) = v m ( x ) for convenience. Note that j = 0 \u2208 H min and j = n + 1 \u2208 H max . It is also important to point out that by omitting inactive constraints from our mathematical analysis we know that each index j = 0 , \u2026 , n + 1 lies in precisely one of the above sets. Define the weighted cost function J w ( u ) = J ( u ) + \u2211 j = 1 n + 1 \u03ba j [ t ( x j ) \u2212 t ( 0 ) ] = \u222b 0 X [ u a \u2212 \u03c1 u b + \u2211 j = 1 n + 1 \u03ba j \u03c7 ( 0 , x j ) ( x ) v ( x ) ] d x where \u03ba = ( \u03ba 1 , \u2026 , \u03ba n + 1 ) is a vector of constant weight factors and \u03c7 ( 0 , x j ) is the indicator function for the open interval x\u202f\u2208\u202f(0, xj ). Importantly we note that \u03baj \u202f>\u202f0 when j\u202f\u2208\u202fH max and \u03baj \u202f<\u202f0 when j\u202f\u2208\u202fH min. It is convenient to rewrite this expression immediately as J w ( u ) = \u222b 0 X [ u a \u2212 \u03c1 u b + \u2211 k = 0 n \u03bb k \u03c7 ( x k , x k + 1 ) ( x ) v ( x ) ] d x where \u03bb = ( \u03bb 0 , \u2026 , \u03bb n ) and we have defined \u03bb k = \u03ba k + 1 + \u22ef + \u03ba n + 1 for each k = 0 , 1 , \u2026 , n . Note that \u03bb k > \u03bb k + 1 \u21d4 \u03ba k + 1 > 0 \u21d4 k + 1 \u2208 H max and \u03bb k < \u03bb k + 1 \u21d4 \u03ba k + 1 < 0 \u21d4 k + 1 \u2208 H min . We will see later that it is necessary to have \u03bbk \u202f>\u202f0 for all k. We use the Pontryagin principle (Hartl et al., 1995) to find necessary conditions on u = u 0 such that J w ( u 0 ) = inf u \u2208 U J w ( u ) where U is the set of feasible control functions. We form the Hamiltonian function H w = ( \u2212 1 ) [ u a \u2212 \u03c1 u b + \u2211 k = 0 n \u03bb k \u03c7 ( x k , x k + 1 ) v ] + \u03bc ( u a \u2212 u b \u2212 r ( v ) + g ) v where \u03bc = \u03bc ( x ) is the adjoint variable and where we suppress the dependence on position by writing u = u ( x ) , v = v ( x ) , g = g ( x ) and \u03c7 ( 0 , x j ) = \u03c7 ( 0 , x j ) ( x ) for convenience. We must maximize H w with respect to the controls ua and ub at each point subject to the control constraints 0\u202f\u2264\u202fua \u202f\u2264\u202fH(v) and 0\u202f\u2264\u202fub \u202f\u2264\u202fK(v) and so we form the associated Lagrangian L w = H w + \u03b1 1 ( H ( v ) \u2212 u a ) + \u03b1 2 u a + \u03b2 1 ( K ( v ) \u2212 u b ) + \u03b2 2 u b = ( \u2212 1 ) [ u a \u2212 \u03c1 u b + \u2211 k = 0 n \u03bb k \u03c7 ( x k , x k + 1 ) v ] + \u03bc ( u a \u2212 u b \u2212 r ( v ) + g ) v + \u03b1 1 ( H ( v ) \u2212 u a ) + \u03b1 2 u a + \u03b2 1 ( K ( v ) \u2212 u b ) + \u03b2 2 u b where \u03b1 1\u202f\u2265\u202f0, \u03b1 2\u202f\u2265\u202f0, \u03b2 1\u202f\u2265\u202f0 and \u03b2 2\u202f\u2265\u202f0 are Lagrange multipliers. The adjoint differential equation is d \u03bc d x = \u2212 \u2202 L w \u2202 v = \u2212 \u2211 k = 0 n \u03bb k \u03c7 ( x k , x k + 1 ) v 2 + \u03bc r \u2032 ( v ) v + \u03bc ( u a \u2212 u b \u2212 r ( v ) + g ) v 2 \u2212 \u03b1 1 H \u2032 ( v ) \u2212 \u03b2 1 K \u2032 ( v ) . To maximize H w with respect to ua and ub subject to the constraints 0\u202f\u2264\u202fua \u202f\u2264\u202fH(v) and 0\u202f\u2264\u202fub \u202f\u2264\u202fK(v) we apply the Karush\u2013Kuhn\u2013Tucker conditions \u2202 L w \u2202 u a = \u03bc v \u2212 1 \u2212 \u03b1 1 + \u03b1 2 = 0 and \u2202 L w \u2202 u b = \u2212 \u03bc v + \u03c1 \u2212 \u03b2 1 + \u03b2 2 = 0 and the complementary slackness conditions \u03b1 1 ( H ( v ) \u2212 u a ) = 0 , \u03b1 2 u a = 0 , \u03b2 1 ( K ( v ) \u2212 u b ) = 0 and \u03b2 2 u b = 0 . By following essentially the same steps that we used in our analysis of the two-train separation problem we reach similar conclusions about the general form for the strategies of optimal type. 16.3 The singular phases in the strategies of optimal type In particular we can show that the optimal regime includes a singular strategy with \u03bc = v , ( \u03b1 1 , \u03b1 2 ) = ( 0 , 0 ) and ( \u03b2 1 , \u03b2 2 ) = ( 0 , 1 \u2212 \u03c1 ) and with ua \u202f\u2208\u202f[0, H(v)] and u b = 0 . This is a partial acceleration phase. If the condition \u03bc = v is maintained over a non-trivial interval then d \u03bc d x = d v d x \u27f9 \u2212 \u2211 k = 0 n \u03bb k \u03c7 ( x k , x k + 1 ) v 2 + r \u2032 ( v ) = 0 \u21d4 \u03c8 ( v ) = \u2211 k = 0 n \u03bb k \u03c7 ( x k , x k + 1 ) where we have again written \u03c8 ( v ) = v 2 r \u2032 ( v ) . For x \u2208 ( x k , x k + 1 ) this equation reduces to \u03c8 ( v ) = \u03bb k and has a unique solution v = V k provided \u03bbk \u202f>\u202f0. Hence the optimal partial acceleration phase on the interval ( x k , x k + 1 ) is a speedhold phase at speed Vk with u a ( x ) = r ( V k ) \u2212 g ( x ) . This strategy can only occur if 0 < r ( V k ) \u2212 g ( x ) < H . We say that Vk is the optimal driving speed on the interval ( x k , x k + 1 ) . Note that either V k > V k + 1 with \u03bb k > \u03bb k + 1 > 0 or else V k < V k + 1 with 0 < \u03bb k < \u03bb k + 1 . The optimal regime also includes a singular strategy with \u03bc = \u03c1 v , ( \u03b1 1 , \u03b1 2 ) = ( 0 , 1 \u2212 \u03c1 ) and ( \u03b2 1 , \u03b2 2 ) = ( 0 , 0 ) and with u a = 0 and ub \u202f\u2208\u202f[0, K(v)]. This is a partial brake phase. If the condition \u03bc = \u03c1 v is maintained over a non-trivial interval then d \u03bc d x = \u03c1 d v d x \u27f9 \u2212 \u2211 k = 0 n \u03bb k \u03c7 ( x k , x k + 1 ) v 2 + \u03c1 r \u2032 ( v ) = 0 \u21d4 \u03c1 \u03c8 ( v ) = \u2211 k = 0 n \u03bb k \u03c7 ( x k , x k + 1 ) For x \u2208 ( x k , x k + 1 ) this equation reduces to \u03c1 \u03c8 ( v ) = \u03bb k and has a unique solution v = W k provided \u03bbk \u202f>\u202f0. Hence the optimal partial brake phase on the interval ( x k , x k + 1 ) is a speedhold phase at speed Wk with u b ( x ) = g ( x ) \u2212 r ( W k ) . We say that Wk is the optimal regenerative braking speed on the interval ( x k , x k + 1 ) . It follows that the partial brake phase can only occur if 0 < g ( x ) \u2212 r ( W k ) < K . Note that the holdspeeds Vk and Wk for partial acceleration and partial brake are related by the formula W k = \u03c8 \u2212 1 [ ( 1 / \u03c1 ) \u03c8 ( V k ) ] . 16.4 Feasibilty of the problem We conclude our discussion of the three-train separation problem with some final remarks about the broader context. Suppose the leading train follows the unrestricted optimal strategy with speed profile v 0 = v 0 ( x ) and starts at time h \u2113 , 0 = 0 . Then we can calculate the times t \u2113 ( x j + 2 ) = h \u2113 , 0 + \u222b 0 x j + 2 d x v 0 ( x ) = h 0 , j + 2 when the leading train passes the signal location x j + 2 for each j = 0 , \u2026 , n \u2212 2 . If the middle train starts at time h m , 0 = \u0394 T m and also follows the unrestricted optimal strategy then we can calculate the times t m ( x j ) = h m , 0 + \u222b 0 x j d x v 0 ( x ) = \u0394 T m + h 0 , j when the middle train passes the signal location xj for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n . However we require t m ( x j ) \u2265 t \u2113 ( x j + 2 ) because the middle train cannot enter section ( x j , x j + 1 ) until the leading train has left section ( x j + 1 , x j + 2 ) . Hence we require \u0394 T m + h 0 , j \u2265 h 0 , j + 2 . Now suppose the following train starts at time h f , 0 = \u0394 T f and also follows an unrestricted optimal strategy. Then the following train passes the location x j \u2212 2 at time t f ( x j \u2212 2 ) = h f , 0 + \u222b 0 x j \u2212 2 d x v 0 ( x ) = \u0394 T f + h 0 , j \u2212 2 for each j = 2 , \u2026 , n . However the following train cannot enter section ( x j \u2212 2 , x j \u2212 1 ) until the middle train has left section ( x j \u2212 1 , x j ) . Thus we must have t f ( x j \u2212 2 ) \u2265 t m ( x j ) . It follows that we require \u0394 T f + h 0 , j \u2212 2 \u2265 t m ( x j ) \u2265 \u0394 T m + h 0 , j . Putting these requirements together with the earlier requirements means that we must have \u0394 T f + h 0 , j \u2212 2 \u2265 \u0394 T m + h 0 , j \u2265 h 0 , j + 2 for each j = 1 , \u2026 , n \u2212 1 . If we want all three trains to follow the unrestricted optimal strategy then it is necessary to choose \u0394 T m \u2265 max j = 0 n \u2212 2 [ h 0 , j + 2 \u2212 h 0 , j ] and \u0394 T f \u2265 \u0394 T m + max j = 2 n [ h 0 , j \u2212 h 0 , j \u2212 2 ] \u2265 max j = 2 n \u2212 2 [ h 0 , j + 2 \u2212 h 0 , j \u2212 2 ] . If we assume that it is not possible for all three trains to follow the unrestricted optimal strategy then to find suitable bounds on the intermediate clearance times we must consider reference strategies for the leading train that satisfy the overall time constraint but are faster in the early part of the journey and reference strategies for the following train that also satisfy the overall time constraint but are slower in the early part of the journey. The arguments are similar to the arguments used in the two-train separation problem but the requirements for the middle train are more complex. 17 Conclusions and future work We have shown that if two trains travel on the same track in the same direction subject to prescribed intermediate section clearance times then the optimal driving strategy for the leading train is uniquely defined by a decreasing sequence of optimal driving speeds and the optimal driving strategy for the following train is uniquely defined by an increasing sequence of optimal driving speeds. We showed that the leading train and following train problems are logically independent and so the optimal strategies and associated speed profiles can be calculated separately. We have used a realistic example and an ad hoc set of prescribed clearance times to show that each strategy can be computed accurately using an iterative process. Each step in the iteration is a bipartite procedure. In the first part a provisional sequence of optimal driving speeds is used to find a speed profile of optimal type and to calculate the associated clearance times at each signal location. In the second part the computed clearance times are compared to the prescribed clearance times and the provisional sequence of optimal driving speeds is then adjusted. In essence we simply increase the relevant optimal driving speeds on those sections where the train is too slow and decrease them on those sections where the train is too fast. Our theoretical solution provides valuable insight into the form of the optimal strategy for each train but there is an urgent need to find efficient numerical algorithms that ensure convergence of the iterative procedure to the desired solution. The current Matlab algorithms still require manual intervention at each stage. A related mathematical problem, which we propose to address in a subsequent paper, is to find necessary conditions for a set of optimal prescribed intermediate section clearance times that minimize the total tractive energy consumed by the two trains over all possible sets of prescribed clearance times. In Example 4 the cost of the optimal strategies was J = J \u2113 + J f \u2248 2153.91 . We expect this cost can be decreased if the specified intermediate times are optimized. Although we believe the necessary conditions for optimized intermediate times on non-level track will be formally the same as they were on level track (Albrecht et al., 2015c; 2015d; 2015e) the mathematical formulae for the required derivatives\u2014already shown in Albrecht et al. (2015e) to be algebraically complicated on level track\u2014will be more so on non-level track. Despite the underlying complexity in calculation of analytic formul\u00e6 for individual derivatives the necessary optimality conditions are relatively easy to apply and were used in Albrecht et al. (2015c) to verify the optimality of a computed solution on level track. The numerical computation is not expected to be problematic for non-level track. At the same time the determination of efficient numerical algorithms to find the optimal intermediate clearance times will be a priority in future research.
